How to use this guide
  • Go in order. Every lesson assumes the ones before it and nothing else. Skipping ahead is the main reason people give up.
  • Read the notes, then close them. Try to explain the idea out loud before moving to the exercises. If you cannot, read it again.
  • Type every exercise yourself. Do not copy-paste, and do not open the solution until you have genuinely tried. Ten minutes of being stuck teaches more than an hour of reading.
  • Do the written worksheets on paper. Explaining something in your own words is the fastest way to discover you did not really understand it.
  • To run the code: install Python from python.org, or use any free online Python editor. Stage 1 Lesson 4 covers the full setup.
  • To print: press Ctrl+P. Solutions and answers are hidden automatically, so it prints as a clean worksheet.
  • Two hours a day, six days a week takes you through all three stages in roughly eight months.
STAGE 1

🌱 Absolute Beginner

You have never written a line of code. Every idea taught from zero in plain everyday language, with worked examples showing every step.

3 tracks · 9 lessons · 71 exercises

🌱 First Steps with Computers

You have never written a single line of code. That is exactly who this is for. By the end you will have made the computer do what YOU told it to do.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Explain in your own words what a program is
  • Write and run your first Python program
  • Store information in variables and use it
  • Ask the user a question and use their answer
Lesson 1

What is a computer really doing?

🌱 First Steps with Computers · 20 min read · 8 exercises · 9 worksheet questions

Let us start with something you already know

Think about making tea. You follow steps:

1. Boil water
2. Add tea leaves
3. Wait 3 minutes
4. Add milk and sugar
5. Pour into cup

That list of steps is called a recipe. You follow it from top to bottom.

A computer program is exactly the same thing. It is a list of steps. The computer starts at the top and does each step, one after another, until it reaches the bottom.

That is it. That is what programming is. Writing steps for the computer to follow.

But the computer is very, very stupid

This part surprises everybody. The computer is not clever. It is extremely fast, but it has no common sense at all.

If you tell a friend "make tea", they know what you mean. If you tell a computer "make tea", it has no idea. You must say every single step. If you forget "add water", the computer will happily try to boil an empty vessel.

Remember this: The computer does exactly what you say. Not what you meant. Almost every mistake you will ever make in programming comes from this one fact.

The four things a computer can do

Every program in the world — games, WhatsApp, YouTube, banking apps — is built from only four actions:

  • Remember something (store a number, a name, a score)
  • Compare two things (is this bigger? are these the same?)
  • Repeat something (do this 100 times)
  • Decide what to do next based on a comparison

Four things. That is the whole toolbox. Everything else is just these four combined in clever ways.

The parts of a computer, in simple words

  • CPU — the brain. It does the thinking, extremely fast. Millions of steps every second.
  • RAM — the desk. Things you are working on right now sit here. When you switch off, the desk is cleared completely.
  • Hard disk — the cupboard. Things stored here stay even after switching off. Your photos and files live here.

This is why you lose an unsaved document when the power goes. It was on the desk (RAM), never put in the cupboard (disk).

What is Python?

The computer only understands electricity — on and off, which we write as 1 and 0. Writing programs in 1s and 0s would be impossible for humans.

So we write in a programming language instead. It looks a bit like English. Then a translator turns it into 1s and 0s for the computer.

Python is one such language. We chose it because it is the closest to plain English, and because it is what real engineers use to build Artificial Intelligence.

Your very first instruction: print

print means "show this on the screen". It does not mean printing on paper.

Worked example 1

print("Hello")

Let us read this slowly, piece by piece:

  • print — the instruction. It says: show something.
  • ( and ) — brackets. Whatever goes inside is what gets shown.
  • "Hello" — the message. The double quotes tell Python "this is text, not an instruction".

Output on screen:

Hello

Worked example 2 — two lines

print("My name is Ravi")
print("I am learning Python")

The computer starts at the top. It does line 1, shows the first message. Then it moves to line 2 and shows the second message.

Output:

My name is Ravi
I am learning Python

Each print starts a new line. You did not have to ask for that; it happens automatically.

Worked example 3 — a common mistake

print(Hello)

This gives an error. The quotes are missing. Without quotes, Python thinks Hello is the name of something you stored earlier. It looks for it, cannot find it, and complains.

Rule: text always goes inside quotes.

Errors are completely normal

You will see errors constantly. Every professional programmer sees errors many times a day, every day, for their whole career. An error is not failure. It is the computer telling you which line it did not understand. Read it, fix that line, run again.

Practice — 8 exercises

Type these out yourself. Do not read the solution until you have genuinely tried. Struggling for ten minutes teaches more than reading for an hour.

1

print("")

# add the second line below

You need two separate print instructions, one under the other.
Show solution

print("I am learning Python")
4

Show hint
Text needs quotes around it. Put " before Good and after morning.
Show solution

Show hint
Three separate lines, each with its own print.
Show solution

print("Go")
6
The computer can do maths too. Numbers do not need quotes. Make it show the answer to 25 + 17.

Show hint

Show solution
print(25 + 17)
7
Now see the difference. Run this and look carefully at the two outputs.
print(10 + 5)
print("10 + 5")
Show hint
Without quotes Python calculates. With quotes it just shows the text exactly.
Show solution

print("10 + 5")
8

# line 2: the division, no quotes

Lesson 2

Boxes that remember: variables

🌱 First Steps with Computers · 25 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

The problem

Say you are writing a program about a student called Anjali. You write her name in ten different places. Then you need to change it to Bhavna. Now you must find and fix all ten places. Miss one, and your program is wrong.

There is a better way.

A variable is a labelled box

Imagine a box. You put something inside it. You stick a label on the front. Later, whenever you say the label, you get whatever is inside.

name = "Anjali"

Read this from right to left, like this:

  • Take the text Anjali
  • Put it in a box
  • Label that box name

The = sign here does not mean "equals" like in maths. It means "put this into that box". Programmers call it assignment.

Worked example 1

name = "Anjali"
print(name)

Step by step:

  • Line 1: box labelled name now holds the text Anjali
  • Line 2: print(name) — no quotes! So Python does not show the word "name". It looks inside the box called name and shows what it finds.

Output:

Anjali
Very important: print(name) shows what is inside the box. print("name") shows the actual word name. The quotes change everything.

Boxes can hold different kinds of things

name  = "Anjali"      # text      — needs quotes
age   = 15            # whole number — no quotes
marks = 87.5          # decimal number — no quotes
passed = True         # yes or no  — no quotes, capital T

These four kinds have names you should learn:

  • string — text. Always in quotes. Short for "a string of letters".
  • integer — a whole number. 5, 100, -3
  • float — a number with a decimal point. 87.5, 3.14
  • boolean — only two possible values: True or False. Capital letters matter.

Anything after a # is a comment. Python ignores it completely. Comments are notes for humans reading the code.

Worked example 2 — changing what is in the box

score = 10
print(score)

score = 25
print(score)

What happens:

  • Box score gets 10. Shows 10.
  • Box score gets 25. The old 10 is thrown away — a box holds only one thing at a time. Shows 25.

Output:

10
25

This is why they are called variables. What is inside can vary — it can change.

Worked example 3 — using boxes in maths

maths = 80
science = 90
total = maths + science
print(total)

Step by step:

  • Box maths holds 80
  • Box science holds 90
  • Python looks in both boxes, adds 80 + 90 to get 170, puts 170 in a new box called total
  • Shows what is in total

Output: 170

Worked example 4 — joining text together

To show text and a variable together nicely, put the letter f before the opening quote, and put variable names inside curly brackets { }:

name = "Anjali"
age = 15
print(f"{name} is {age} years old")

Output:

Anjali is 15 years old

This is called an f-string (f for "format"). It is the easiest way to mix text and variables. Use it always.

Rules for naming your boxes

  • Use lowercase letters: name, not Name
  • No spaces. Use underscore instead: first_name
  • Cannot start with a number: 2marks is wrong, marks2 is fine
  • Choose names that explain themselves. x tells the reader nothing. total_marks tells them everything.
Why good names matter: In three weeks you will read your own code and have forgotten what you meant. Good names are a message to your future self.

Practice — 8 exercises

Type these out yourself. Do not read the solution until you have genuinely tried. Struggling for ten minutes teaches more than reading for an hour.

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
What is a variable? Explain using the box idea.
Show answer
A variable is like a box with a label. You put a value inside, and whenever you use the label, you get the value that is inside.
2
In programming, what does the = sign mean? Is it the same as in maths?
Show answer
No. In programming it means 'put this value into that box'. It is called assignment. In maths it means both sides are equal.
3
What is the difference between print(name) and print("name")?
Show answer
print(name) opens the box called name and shows what is inside. print("name") shows the actual word 'name' because the quotes make it plain text.
4
Name the four types and give one example of each.
Show answer
string (text) - "Anjali" integer (whole number) - 15 float (decimal number) - 87.5 boolean (true or false) - True
5
Which of these variable names are wrong, and why? a) first_name b) 2marks c) total marks d) Score
Show answer
b) 2marks is wrong - cannot start with a number. c) total marks is wrong - no spaces allowed, use total_marks. a) and d) both work, but lowercase is the normal style, so first_name is better than Score.
6
What does the # symbol do?
Show answer
It makes a comment. Python ignores everything after it on that line. Comments are notes for humans reading the code.
7
What will this show? x = 5 x = 9 print(x)
Show answer
9. A box holds only one thing at a time. Putting 9 in throws away the 5.
8
Write code that stores your age in a box and shows the sentence: I am __ years old
Show answer
age = 15 print(f"I am {age} years old")
9
Why is total_marks a better variable name than t?
Show answer
Because it explains itself. Anyone reading the code, including you in three weeks, immediately knows what it holds. t tells you nothing.
10
A shop sells pens at 12 rupees each. Write code to find the cost of 7 pens and show it.
Show answer
price = 12 quantity = 7 total = price * quantity print(total) Answer: 84
Lesson 3

Talking to the user: input

🌱 First Steps with Computers · 25 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

So far your programs are boring

Everything you have written does the same thing every single time. Real programs are different — they respond to the person using them.

To do that, the program must be able to ask a question and receive an answer.

The input instruction

name = input("What is your name? ")

What happens, step by step:

  • The program shows the question on screen: What is your name?
  • It stops and waits. Nothing else happens until the user types something and presses Enter.
  • Whatever they typed goes into the box called name

Worked example 1

name = input("What is your name? ")
print(f"Hello {name}, nice to meet you!")

If the user types Priya, the output is:

What is your name? Priya
Hello Priya, nice to meet you!

Notice the space after the question mark inside the quotes. Without it, the user's typing would touch the question mark and look cramped. Small detail, but it matters.

The big trap: input always gives you text

This catches every single beginner. Read it twice.

Whatever the user types, input hands it to you as text — even if they typed a number.

age = input("Your age? ")
print(age + 1)

If the user types 15, this gives an error. Why? Because age holds the text "15", not the number 15. And you cannot add 1 to a piece of text. Python does not know what "15" + 1 should mean.

The fix: int() converts text into a number

age = int(input("Your age? "))
print(age + 1)

Now if the user types 15, the program shows 16.

Read the line from the inside out:

  • input("Your age? ") runs first — gets the text "15"
  • int( ... ) wraps around it — turns the text "15" into the number 15
  • age = ... puts the number 15 into the box

Worked example 2 — adding two numbers from the user

first = int(input("First number: "))
second = int(input("Second number: "))
total = first + second
print(f"The total is {total}")

If the user types 20 and then 35:

First number: 20
Second number: 35
The total is 55

Try removing both int wrappers and running it. With input 20 and 35 you get 2035 — because joining two pieces of text just sticks them together. This one experiment will teach you more than any explanation.

Three conversion tools

  • int() — text to whole number. int("15") gives 15
  • float() — text to decimal number. float("87.5") gives 87.5
  • str() — number to text. str(15) gives "15"

Use int() for ages, counts, marks out of a whole number. Use float() for anything that might have a decimal point, like a price or a percentage.

Worked example 3 — a small calculator

print("Rectangle area calculator")
length = float(input("Length in cm: "))
breadth = float(input("Breadth in cm: "))
area = length * breadth
print(f"The area is {area} square cm")

With inputs 5 and 4:

Rectangle area calculator
Length in cm: 5
Breadth in cm: 4
The area is 20.0 square cm

You have now written a genuinely useful tool. It works for any rectangle, not just one.

A note about the exercises here: The practice box cannot type answers for you. So in these exercises we will set the value directly instead of using input, exactly as if the user had typed it. The thinking is identical.

Practice — 8 exercises

Type these out yourself. Do not read the solution until you have genuinely tried. Struggling for ten minutes teaches more than reading for an hour.

# line 1: the total
# line 2: the average
Show hint
total = int(m1)+int(m2)+int(m3) gives 255. Then average = total/3 gives 85.0
Show solution

total = int(m1) + int(m2) + int(m3)
print(total)
print(total / 3)
8
Challenge: age "12" as text. Show the sentence In 5 years you will be 17.
age = "12"
# convert, add 5, use an f-string
Show hint
future = int(age) + 5, then print(f"In 5 years you will be {future}")
Show solution

print(f"In 5 years you will be {future}")

Show answer
It shows a question on screen, waits for the user to type something and press Enter, then gives you whatever they typed.

What type of thing does input ALWAYS give you, even when the user types a number?
Show answer
Text (a string). Never a number, even if it looks like one.
3

Show answer
Because age holds text, not a number. You cannot add the number 1 to a piece of text. Python does not know what that should mean.

How do you fix the code above?
Show answer
Wrap the input in int(): age = int(input("Age? ")) print(age + 1)
5
What do int(), float() and str() each do?
Show answer
int() turns text into a whole number. float() turns text into a decimal number. str() turns a number into text.
6
A user types 20 and 35 into a program that forgot to convert. The answer shown is 2035. Explain why.
Show answer
Both values are still text. Adding two pieces of text joins them end to end instead of doing maths. So "20" + "35" becomes "2035".
7
Should you use int() or float() for a price like 49.50? Why?
Show answer
float(), because the price has a decimal point. int() would refuse it or lose the paise.

Show answer
num = int(input("Enter a number: ")) print(num * 2)

Show answer
So the user's typing does not touch the question mark. Without the space it looks cramped: 'What is your name?Priya'

🧠 Making Programs Think

Right now your programs do the same thing every time. Now you will teach them to make decisions and to repeat work — which is where programming gets genuinely powerful.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Make the program choose between different actions
  • Repeat work automatically instead of copying lines
  • Store many values together in a list
  • Combine all of these to solve real problems
Lesson 4

Making decisions: if and else

🧠 Making Programs Think · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

You do this every day

"If it is raining, take an umbrella. Otherwise, do not."

You made a decision by checking a condition. Programs do exactly this.

The if statement

marks = 75

if marks >= 40:
    print("Pass")

Reading it: if marks is 40 or more, show Pass.

Since marks is 75, and 75 is more than 40, it shows Pass.

If marks had been 30, nothing at all would happen. The program would skip that line completely.

Two things you must get exactly right

1. The colon. There is a : at the end of the if line. Forget it and you get an error.

2. The indentation. The print line is pushed in by four spaces. This is not decoration — it is how Python knows which lines belong to the if.

if marks >= 40:
    print("Pass")          <-- indented, so this only runs if the condition is true
print("Program over")      <-- not indented, so this ALWAYS runs
In most other languages you use curly brackets { } for this. Python uses spaces instead, which makes code cleaner to read but means you must be careful. Always use exactly four spaces.

Comparing things

a == b     is a the same as b?      (TWO equal signs!)
a != b     is a different from b?
a >  b     is a bigger than b?
a <  b     is a smaller than b?
a >= b     is a bigger than or equal to b?
a <= b     is a smaller than or equal to b?
The single most common beginner mistake: using one = when you mean ==.
One = means "put this in the box".
Two == means "are these the same?"
In an if statement you almost always want two.

Worked example 1 — if and else

marks = 35

if marks >= 40:
    print("Pass")
else:
    print("Fail")

Step by step:

  • Is 35 greater than or equal to 40? No.
  • So Python skips the first block and runs the else block instead.

Output: Fail

else means "otherwise". It runs only when the if condition was false. Exactly one of the two blocks will run — never both, never neither.

Worked example 2 — more than two choices with elif

marks = 82

if marks >= 90:
    print("Grade A")
elif marks >= 75:
    print("Grade B")
elif marks >= 60:
    print("Grade C")
else:
    print("Grade D")

elif is short for "else if". Python checks each condition in order from the top and stops at the first one that is true.

  • Is 82 >= 90? No. Move on.
  • Is 82 >= 75? Yes! Show Grade B and stop checking entirely.

Output: Grade B

The order matters enormously. If you put marks >= 60 first, then 82 would match it and print Grade C, which is wrong. Always go from the strictest condition to the loosest.

Worked example 3 — checking two things at once

age = 16
has_ticket = True

if age >= 12 and has_ticket:
    print("You may enter")
else:
    print("Entry not allowed")
  • and — BOTH conditions must be true
  • or — at least ONE must be true
  • not — flips true to false and false to true

Here age is 16 (true) and has_ticket is True, so both are satisfied.

Output: You may enter

Worked example 4 — an if inside an if

marks = 85
attendance = 60

if marks >= 40:
    if attendance >= 75:
        print("Promoted")
    else:
        print("Passed exam but attendance too low")
else:
    print("Failed")

The inner if only gets checked when the outer one was true. Notice the inner block is indented eight spaces — four for being inside the outer if, four more for being inside the inner one.

Output: Passed exam but attendance too low

Practice — 8 exercises

Type these out yourself. Do not read the solution until you have genuinely tried. Struggling for ten minutes teaches more than reading for an hour.

1
If marks is 40 or more, show Pass. Marks are 65 here.

if marks >= 40:
    # your print here
    pass
Show hint
Replace the word pass with print("Pass"). Keep the four spaces of indentation.

if marks >= 40:
    print("Pass")

Now add an else. Marks are 25, so it should show Fail.

    print("Pass")
# add else here

Show solution

if marks >= 40:
    print("Pass")
else:
    print("Fail")

    print("Correct")

if answer == "yes":
    print("Correct")

# if number % 2 == 0 then Even, otherwise Odd

    print("Even")

Grades with elif. 90+ is A, 75+ is B, 60+ is C, below that is D. Marks are 78.

# if / elif / elif / else

    print("A")
elif marks >= 75:

top_maths = False

# show Prize if either is True, otherwise No prize
Show hint

Show solution

if top_maths or top_science:
    print("Prize")
else:
    print("No prize")

Challenge: find the largest of three numbers 34, 78 and 56, and show it.
a, b, c = 34, 78, 56

# work out which is biggest and show it
Show hint

Show solution
a, b, c = 34, 78, 56
if a >= b and a >= c:
    print(a)

    print(b)

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
What is the difference between = and == ?
Show answer
= puts a value into a box (assignment). == asks whether two things are the same (comparison). In an if statement you almost always want ==
2
Why does Python need indentation?
Show answer
Indentation tells Python which lines belong inside the if. Lines pushed in by four spaces only run when the condition is true. Lines not indented always run.
3
What does elif mean and when do you use it?
Show answer
It is short for 'else if'. Use it when you have more than two possible outcomes. Python checks each in order and stops at the first true one.
4
What will this show? Explain why. marks = 95 if marks >= 60: print("C") elif marks >= 90: print("A")
Show answer
It shows C, which is wrong. Python checks top to bottom and stops at the first true condition. 95 >= 60 is true, so it never reaches the check for 90. The conditions are in the wrong order - always go strictest first.
5
Explain and, or and not in your own words.
Show answer
and - both conditions must be true or - at least one must be true not - flips the answer around

Always. 'Done' is not indented, so it is not inside the if. It runs no matter what x is.
Lesson 5

Repeating work: loops

🧠 Making Programs Think · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

The problem loops solve

Suppose you want to show the numbers 1 to 5. You could write:

print(1)
print(2)
print(3)
print(4)
print(5)

Tedious, but fine. Now show 1 to 1000. You are not going to type a thousand lines.

This is what loops are for. A loop lets you write the instruction once and tell the computer how many times to do it.

The for loop

for i in range(5):
    print(i)

Output:

0
1
2
3
4

Reading it piece by piece:

  • for — start a loop
  • i — a box that holds the current number. It changes each time round.
  • range(5) — gives five numbers, starting from 0: 0, 1, 2, 3, 4
  • : and indentation — same rules as if
Why does it start at 0 and not 1? This confuses everybody at first. Computers have counted from 0 since the very beginning, for reasons to do with how memory addresses work. range(5) gives you five numbers, but they are 0 to 4, not 1 to 5. You will get used to it faster than you expect.

Getting the numbers you actually want

range(5)         gives  0, 1, 2, 3, 4
range(1, 6)      gives  1, 2, 3, 4, 5      (starts at 1, stops BEFORE 6)
range(1, 11)     gives  1 to 10
range(0, 10, 2)  gives  0, 2, 4, 6, 8      (the 2 means: count in steps of 2)

With two numbers, the first is where to start and the second is where to stop — and it always stops before the second number, never on it.

Worked example 1 — the 7 times table

for i in range(1, 11):
    print(f"7 x {i} = {7 * i}")

Output:

7 x 1 = 7
7 x 2 = 14
7 x 3 = 21
... and so on to ...
7 x 10 = 70

Two lines of code did the work of ten. That is the point of loops.

Worked example 2 — adding up numbers

total = 0

for i in range(1, 6):
    total = total + i

print(total)

Let us trace it carefully, round by round:

Start:        total = 0
Round 1: i=1  total = 0 + 1 = 1
Round 2: i=2  total = 1 + 2 = 3
Round 3: i=3  total = 3 + 3 = 6
Round 4: i=4  total = 6 + 4 = 10
Round 5: i=5  total = 10 + 5 = 15
Loop ends
print shows 15

Notice print is not indented. It sits outside the loop, so it runs once at the end. If you indented it, you would see the running total five times instead.

Tracing code like this — writing down what each variable holds after each round — is the single most useful debugging skill you can learn. When a loop misbehaves, trace it on paper.

Worked example 3 — looping over words

Loops are not only for numbers:

for fruit in ["mango", "apple", "banana"]:
    print(f"I like {fruit}")

Output:

I like mango
I like apple
I like banana

The box fruit holds a different item each time round.

The while loop

A for loop runs a known number of times. A while loop keeps going until something changes.

count = 1

while count <= 3:
    print(count)
    count = count + 1

print("Finished")

Output:

1
2
3
Finished

Before each round, Python checks: is count still 3 or less? When count reaches 4, the answer is no, and the loop stops.

Danger — the infinite loop. If you forget the line count = count + 1, count stays at 1 forever, the condition is always true, and the program never stops. Your screen fills with 1s until you force it to quit. Every programmer has done this. Always check: does something inside the loop change the condition?

Worked example 4 — a loop inside a loop

for i in range(1, 4):
    for j in range(1, 4):
        print(f"{i} x {j} = {i * j}")

The outer loop runs 3 times. For each of those, the inner loop runs 3 times. So you get 3 x 3 = 9 lines in total.

Think of it like a clock: the outer loop is the hour hand, the inner loop is the minute hand. The minutes complete a full cycle for every single hour.

Practice — 8 exercises

Type these out yourself. Do not read the solution until you have genuinely tried. Struggling for ten minutes teaches more than reading for an hour.

1
Show the numbers 0 to 4, one per line.
for i in range(5):
    # show i here
    pass
Show hint
Replace pass with print(i)
Show solution
for i in range(5):
    print(i)
2
Now show 1 to 5 instead. You will need two numbers in range.
for i in range(5):    # fix this
    print(i)
Show hint

Show solution
for i in range(1, 6):
    print(i)
3
Show the 3 times table from 3 x 1 to 3 x 5, in the form 3 x 1 = 3
for i in range(1, 6):
    # use an f-string
    pass
Show hint

    print(f"3 x {i} = {3 * i}")

    # add i to total
    pass

    print(i)

    # your print here
    pass

Use a while loop to count down from 3 to 1, then show Go.

    print(count)
    # what is missing here?

    count = count - 1

8
Challenge: count how many numbers between 1 and 20 divide exactly by 3, and show the count.

    # if i divides by 3 with no remainder, add 1 to count
    pass
print(count)
Show hint
if i % 3 == 0: count = count + 1. The numbers are 3,6,9,12,15,18 so the answer is 6.
Show solution
count = 0
for i in range(1, 21):
    if i % 3 == 0:
        count = count + 1
print(count)

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Why do we use loops instead of writing the same line many times?
Show answer
So we can write an instruction once and tell the computer how many times to repeat it. Writing 1000 lines by hand is impossible; a loop does it in two lines.
2
What numbers does range(5) give?
Show answer
0, 1, 2, 3, 4 - five numbers, but starting from 0, not 1.
3
What numbers does range(1, 6) give?
Show answer
1, 2, 3, 4, 5 - it starts at the first number and stops BEFORE the second.
4
What does the third number in range(0, 10, 2) do?
Show answer
It is the step - how much to count by. This gives 0, 2, 4, 6, 8.
5
What is the difference between a for loop and a while loop?
Show answer
A for loop runs a known number of times. A while loop keeps going until a condition stops being true.
6
What is an infinite loop and how do you accidentally create one?
Show answer
A loop that never stops. It happens when nothing inside the loop changes the condition - for example forgetting count = count + 1 in a while loop.
7
Trace this code. Write down total after each round. total = 0 for i in range(1, 4): total = total + i print(total)
Show answer
Start: total = 0 Round 1: i=1, total = 1 Round 2: i=2, total = 3 Round 3: i=3, total = 6 Output: 6
8
Write a loop that shows the 5 times table from 5x1 to 5x10.
Show answer
for i in range(1, 11): print(f"5 x {i} = {5 * i}")
9
What is the difference between putting print inside the loop versus outside it?
Show answer
Inside (indented) it runs every round. Outside (not indented) it runs once, after the loop has completely finished.
10
How many lines will this print in total? Explain. for i in range(1, 4): for j in range(1, 4): print(i, j)
Show answer
9 lines. The outer loop runs 3 times, and for each of those the inner loop runs 3 times. 3 x 3 = 9.
Lesson 6

Many things in one box: lists

🧠 Making Programs Think · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

The problem

You want to store the marks of 5 students. So far you would write:

mark1 = 78
mark2 = 92
mark3 = 65
mark4 = 88
mark5 = 71

Now do it for 50 students. Or 500. This does not work.

A list is a box with many compartments

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]

One box called marks, holding five values in order. Square brackets [ ] around them, commas between them.

Getting one item out

Each item has a position number, called its index. Counting starts at 0.

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]
          0   1   2   3   4      <-- these are the index numbers

print(marks[0])    shows 78    (the first item)
print(marks[2])    shows 65    (the third item)
print(marks[-1])   shows 71    (the LAST item, counting backwards)
The most common list error: A list with 5 items has indexes 0, 1, 2, 3 and 4. There is no index 5. Asking for marks[5] gives an "index out of range" error. The last index is always one less than the number of items.

Useful things you can do

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]

len(marks)          how many items?     gives 5
sum(marks)          add them all up     gives 394
max(marks)          the biggest         gives 92
min(marks)          the smallest        gives 65
sum(marks)/len(marks)   the average     gives 78.8

These four built-in tools do a lot of the work for you.

Worked example 1 — the class average

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]

total = sum(marks)
count = len(marks)
average = total / count

print(f"Total: {total}")
print(f"Average: {average}")

Output:

Total: 394
Average: 78.8

Changing a list

fruits = ["mango", "apple"]

fruits.append("banana")     add to the end
fruits[0] = "papaya"        replace the first item
fruits.remove("apple")      remove that item
fruits.sort()               put in order
print(fruits)

append is the one you will use most. It adds a new item to the end of the list.

Worked example 2 — building a list with a loop

squares = []                    start with an empty list

for i in range(1, 6):
    squares.append(i * i)       add each square to the list

print(squares)

Tracing it:

Start:        squares = []
Round 1: i=1  squares = [1]
Round 2: i=2  squares = [1, 4]
Round 3: i=3  squares = [1, 4, 9]
Round 4: i=4  squares = [1, 4, 9, 16]
Round 5: i=5  squares = [1, 4, 9, 16, 25]

Output: [1, 4, 9, 16, 25]

This pattern — start empty, loop, append — is one you will use constantly.

Worked example 3 — looping through a list

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]

for m in marks:
    if m >= 75:
        print(f"{m} - Good")
    else:
        print(f"{m} - Needs work")

Output:

78 - Good
92 - Good
65 - Needs work
88 - Good
71 - Needs work

Here you have combined all three ideas: a list, a loop, and a decision. This is real programming.

Worked example 4 — counting things that match

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]
passed = 0

for m in marks:
    if m >= 75:
        passed = passed + 1

print(f"{passed} out of {len(marks)} scored 75 or above")

Output: 3 out of 5 scored 75 or above

The pattern here: set a counter to 0 before the loop, add 1 inside the loop when the condition matches, show the counter after. Learn this shape — it answers a huge number of questions.

Practice — 8 exercises

Type these out yourself. Do not read the solution until you have genuinely tried. Struggling for ten minutes teaches more than reading for an hour.

Counting starts at 0, so the first item is marks[0]

    # append i times i

Loop through the marks. For each, show Pass if 40 or more, else Fail, one per line.

    # if / else here
    pass
Show hint

Show solution
marks = [55, 30, 80, 25]
for m in marks:
    if m >= 40:
        print("Pass")
    else:
        print("Fail")
8
Challenge: count how many marks are 75 or above, and show just that number.
marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71, 45, 96]
count = 0
# loop, check, count
print(count)
Show hint
Inside the loop: if m >= 75: count = count + 1. The answer is 4.
Show solution
marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71, 45, 96]
count = 0
for m in marks:
    if m >= 75:
        count = count + 1
print(count)

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
What is a list and why is it better than making 50 separate variables?
Show answer
A list is one box holding many values in order. With 50 separate variables you would need 50 names and could not loop over them. A list lets you store any number and process them all with one loop.
2
In the list [10, 20, 30, 40], what is the index of the value 30?
Show answer
2. Counting starts at 0, so 10 is index 0, 20 is index 1, 30 is index 2.
3
A list has 6 items. What is the index of the last one?
Show answer
5. The last index is always one less than the number of items.
4
What does marks[-1] give you?
Show answer
The last item in the list. Negative indexes count backwards from the end.
5
What do len(), sum(), max() and min() do?
Show answer
len() - how many items sum() - adds them all up max() - the biggest min() - the smallest
6
How would you find the average of a list called marks?
Show answer
sum(marks) / len(marks)
7
What does append do?
Show answer
Adds a new item to the end of the list.
8
Trace this. What is in squares at the end? squares = [] for i in range(1, 4): squares.append(i * i)
Show answer
Round 1: i=1, squares = [1] Round 2: i=2, squares = [1, 4] Round 3: i=3, squares = [1, 4, 9] Final: [1, 4, 9]
9
Write code to count how many numbers in a list are greater than 50.
Show answer
count = 0 for n in numbers: if n > 50: count = count + 1 print(count)
10
Why does asking for marks[5] fail when the list has exactly 5 items?
Show answer
Because the indexes are 0, 1, 2, 3, 4. There is no index 5. This gives an 'index out of range' error.

🚀 Build Things & Meet AI

Put everything together to build real working programs. Then find out what Artificial Intelligence actually is, without the hype.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Write your own functions to organise code
  • Build complete working programs from scratch
  • Explain in plain words what AI really is and is not
  • Know exactly what to learn next
Lesson 7

Your own instructions: functions

🚀 Build Things & Meet AI · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

The problem

Suppose you need to calculate the average of marks in five different places in your program. You copy the same three lines five times. Then you find a mistake in the calculation. Now you have five places to fix, and you will miss one.

A function is an instruction you invent yourself

You already use functions: print(), len(), sum(). Someone wrote those for you. Now you write your own.

def greet():
    print("Hello!")
    print("Welcome to Python")

Piece by piece:

  • def — short for "define". It means: I am creating a new instruction.
  • greet — the name you are giving it. You choose this.
  • () — brackets. Information goes in here (empty for now).
  • : and indentation — same rules as always.

Writing the function does not run it. It only teaches Python what greet means. To actually run it, you call it:

greet()

Output:

Hello!
Welcome to Python

Now you can call greet() a hundred times and it does the same thing each time.

Giving the function information

def greet(name):
    print(f"Hello {name}!")

greet("Asha")
greet("Ravi")

Output:

Hello Asha!
Hello Ravi!

The word name inside the brackets is called a parameter. It is an empty box that gets filled with whatever you pass in when you call the function.

Worked example 1 — sending back an answer

Most useful functions calculate something and give it back to you. That is what return does.

def add(a, b):
    answer = a + b
    return answer

result = add(5, 3)
print(result)

Step by step:

  • You call add(5, 3)
  • Inside the function, a becomes 5 and b becomes 3
  • It works out 5 + 3 = 8
  • return answer sends the 8 back out
  • That 8 lands in the box result

Output: 8

print versus return — this trips up everybody.
print shows something on screen. That is all it does. The value is gone afterwards.
return hands the value back so the rest of your program can use it.
A function that prints is a dead end. A function that returns can be built on.

Worked example 2 — a useful function

def average(numbers):
    total = sum(numbers)
    count = len(numbers)
    return total / count

class_a = [78, 92, 65]
class_b = [88, 71, 95, 60]

print(average(class_a))
print(average(class_b))

Output:

78.33333333333333
78.5

You wrote the calculation once. Now it works on any list you give it, forever. If you ever need to fix it, there is exactly one place to look.

Worked example 3 — a function that decides

def grade(marks):
    if marks >= 90:
        return "A"
    elif marks >= 75:
        return "B"
    elif marks >= 60:
        return "C"
    else:
        return "D"

print(grade(95))
print(grade(80))
print(grade(45))

Output:

A
B
D

Important detail: return immediately ends the function. The moment it hits return "A", it stops and sends A back. It never checks the other conditions.

What makes a good function

  • It does one thing. A function called average should calculate an average, not also print a report and save a file.
  • Its name says what it does. calculate_total is good. doStuff is not.
  • It returns rather than prints, unless showing something is genuinely its whole job.

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
What is a function?
Show answer
An instruction you write yourself, with a name. Once written, you can use it as many times as you like by calling its name.
2
What does def mean?
Show answer
Short for 'define'. It tells Python you are creating a new instruction.
3
Does writing a function make it run?
Show answer
No. Writing it only teaches Python what it means. To actually run it you must call it, like greet()
4
What is a parameter?
Show answer
An empty box inside the function's brackets that gets filled with whatever value you pass in when you call it.
5
Explain the difference between print and return.
Show answer
print shows something on screen and then the value is gone. return hands the value back to the rest of the program so it can be stored or used further. A function that prints is a dead end; one that returns can be built upon.
6
What happens the moment a function hits a return statement?
Show answer
It stops immediately and sends that value back. Any code after it in the function never runs.
7
Write a function that returns the square of a number.
Show answer
def square(n): return n * n
8
Write a function that takes a list and returns the largest value, without using max().
Show answer
def largest(numbers): biggest = numbers[0] for n in numbers: if n > biggest: biggest = n return biggest
9
Name three things that make a function good.
Show answer
1. It does one thing only 2. Its name explains what it does 3. It returns a value rather than printing, unless printing is its whole purpose
10
Why is a function better than copying the same three lines five times?
Show answer
If you find a mistake, there is only one place to fix it. With five copies you must fix all five and you will miss one.
Lesson 8

Build real programs

🚀 Build Things & Meet AI · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

You now know enough to build things

Look at what you have learned: variables, input, if/else, loops, lists, functions. That is genuinely the core of programming. Everything else is built on these six ideas.

This lesson is about putting them together.

How to approach any programming problem

Beginners open the editor and start typing. That is the slowest way. Do this instead:

  • Step 1 — Write the steps in plain English first. On paper. No code.
  • Step 2 — Work out what to store. What variables do you need?
  • Step 3 — Write one small piece and test it. Do not write the whole thing then run it.
  • Step 4 — Add the next piece and test again.

Writing a hundred lines then running it for the first time means a hundred lines of possible mistakes. Writing five lines and testing means the mistake is in those five lines.

Worked example 1 — a marks report

Step 1, plain English:

1. Have a list of marks
2. Work out the total
3. Work out the average
4. Find the highest and lowest
5. Count how many passed
6. Show a neat report

Step 2, the code:

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 45, 71]

total = sum(marks)
average = total / len(marks)
highest = max(marks)
lowest = min(marks)

passed = 0
for m in marks:
    if m >= 40:
        passed = passed + 1

print("--- CLASS REPORT ---")
print(f"Students:  {len(marks)}")
print(f"Total:     {total}")
print(f"Average:   {average:.1f}")
print(f"Highest:   {highest}")
print(f"Lowest:    {lowest}")
print(f"Passed:    {passed}")

Output:

--- CLASS REPORT ---
Students:  6
Total:     439
Average:   73.2
Highest:   92
Lowest:    45
Passed:    6

The :.1f inside the curly brackets means "show this number with 1 digit after the decimal point". Without it you would see 73.16666666666667, which looks messy.

Worked example 2 — a number guessing game

secret = 7
guesses = [3, 9, 7]        pretend these are what the user typed

for guess in guesses:
    if guess == secret:
        print(f"{guess} - Correct!")
    elif guess < secret:
        print(f"{guess} - Too low")
    else:
        print(f"{guess} - Too high")

Output:

3 - Too low
9 - Too high
7 - Correct!

A real version would use input() inside a while loop that keeps going until the guess is right.

Worked example 3 — a shopping bill

items = ["Notebook", "Pen", "Eraser"]
prices = [40, 12, 5]
quantities = [3, 5, 2]

total = 0

print("--- BILL ---")
for i in range(len(items)):
    cost = prices[i] * quantities[i]
    total = total + cost
    print(f"{items[i]} x{quantities[i]} = {cost}")

print(f"TOTAL: {total}")

Output:

--- BILL ---
Notebook x3 = 120
Pen x5 = 60
Eraser x2 = 10
TOTAL: 190

The new trick here is range(len(items)). It gives 0, 1, 2 — the index numbers. That lets you reach into all three lists at the same position, so item 0 pairs with price 0 and quantity 0.

When your program does not work

It will not work the first time. That is normal, not a sign of failure. Here is how to find the problem:

  • Read the error's last line. It tells you the line number and what went wrong.
  • Add print statements everywhere. Put print(variable_name) at different points to see what is actually inside your boxes. Usually you will find something is not what you assumed.
  • Trace on paper. Write down each variable's value after each line. Slow, but it finds bugs nothing else will.
  • Check the common ones: = instead of ==, forgotten colon, wrong indentation, text where a number should be.
Frustration is part of the process, not a sign you are bad at this. Every programmer, at every level, spends a large part of their time confused about why something does not work. The difference between a beginner and an expert is not that experts avoid bugs — it is that they have seen more of them before.

Lesson 9

What Artificial Intelligence really is

🚀 Build Things & Meet AI · 30 min read · 7 exercises · 11 worksheet questions

Let us clear up the confusion first

You hear "AI" everywhere. Films show robots that think and feel. News says AI will take everyone's jobs. Some people say it is magic; others say it is dangerous.

Most of that is noise. Here is what is actually true.

Normal programs versus AI

In every program you have written so far, you wrote the rules:

if marks >= 40:
    print("Pass")

You decided that 40 is the pass mark. The computer just followed your instruction.

AI is different. With AI, the computer works out the rules by itself, by looking at lots of examples.

An example that makes it clear

Suppose you want a program that tells cats apart from dogs in photographs.

The normal way would be to write rules: if the ears are pointed and the face is round and the whiskers are long, then it is a cat. You would fail. There are millions of cats and they all look different. Some dogs have pointed ears too. You could write rules for years and never finish.

The AI way: show the computer 50,000 photos of cats and 50,000 photos of dogs, each one labelled. The computer looks for patterns by itself. Nobody tells it what to look for. After enough examples, it can look at a photo it has never seen before and say "cat" — usually correctly.

That is the whole idea. Instead of writing the rules, you show examples and let the computer find the rules. This is called machine learning, and it is what almost everyone means when they say "AI".

How the learning actually happens

You already understand loops, so you can understand this. The computer repeats four steps, millions of times:

  • 1. Guess. At the start the guesses are random and terrible.
  • 2. Check. Compare the guess with the correct answer.
  • 3. Measure how wrong it was. This number is called the loss.
  • 4. Adjust slightly in the direction that would have been less wrong.

Then repeat. Millions of times. Each round the guesses get very slightly better. That is all "training a model" means.

Think of learning to shoot a basketball. First shot misses to the left. You adjust slightly right. Next shot is closer. You keep adjusting. Nobody gives you a formula — you learn from the gap between what you did and what you wanted. A computer learns the same way, just much faster and with numbers instead of muscles.

Where you already meet AI every day

  • YouTube recommendations — learned from what millions of people watched next
  • Google Photos finding your face — learned from labelled face examples
  • Voice typing in your language — learned from thousands of hours of recorded speech
  • Spam filters — learned from emails people marked as spam
  • Google Maps traffic — learned from millions of phones moving along roads
  • ChatGPT and similar — learned from an enormous amount of text

What about ChatGPT?

This one is worth understanding properly, because it is so widely misunderstood.

A chatbot like ChatGPT does exactly one thing: it predicts what word should come next.

If you type "The capital of India is", it has seen that pattern countless times in text, and the word that almost always follows is "Delhi". So it says Delhi. Then it predicts the next word after that, and the next, one at a time.

Do that well enough and it can write essays, answer questions and produce code.

This is why chatbots sometimes state wrong things very confidently. They are not looking up facts in a database. They are predicting what text sounds most likely. Usually the likely-sounding answer is also correct — but not always. This is called hallucination, and it cannot be completely fixed, only reduced. Always check anything important against a real source.

What AI genuinely cannot do

Cutting through the film-hype:

  • It does not understand anything. It finds patterns in numbers. When a chatbot writes "I am happy to help", it does not feel happy. It has no feelings at all.
  • It cannot think about things it never saw. It only knows patterns from its training examples.
  • It is not conscious and does not want anything. It has no goals, no plans, no self.
  • It copies our mistakes. If the examples it learned from were unfair, its answers will be unfair too. This is called bias, and it is a serious real-world problem — for example, a hiring system trained on past hiring that favoured one group will keep favouring that group.

Will AI take everyone's jobs?

Here is an honest answer rather than a scary or a comforting one.

AI is very good at repetitive tasks with clear patterns — sorting documents, basic data entry, simple translation. Those jobs are genuinely changing.

AI is poor at anything needing judgement, real understanding of people, physical skill in messy environments, or responsibility for a decision that matters.

What has actually happened with every major technology — printing, electricity, computers — is that some jobs disappeared, many changed, and new ones appeared that nobody had imagined. Calculators did not end mathematics. But people who understood calculators did better than people who refused to learn them.

The useful position is not to fear it or worship it, but to understand how it works — which you now do better than most adults.

Where to go next

You have finished the school track. You genuinely understand programming basics and what AI is. If you want to keep going:

  • Practise what you know. Build small programs for real things — a marks calculator for your class, a quiz game, a shopping list app. Building beats reading.
  • Then move to the main tracks on this site. They cover Python in depth, then databases, then machine learning properly. They are written for older students, but you now have the foundation to follow them.
  • Learn maths seriously. Especially anything involving averages, percentages, graphs and probability. AI is built on these. Your school maths is not separate from this — it is the actual foundation.

You started this track not knowing what a program was. You can now write one that makes decisions, repeats work, handles lists of data and organises itself into functions. That is a real skill and you built it in nine lessons.

5
Bias - the most important real-world problem.
A company builds an AI to pick which job applicants to interview.
It learns from the company's hiring decisions over the last 20 years.
During those 20 years, the company mostly hired men.

What will the AI do, and why?

Your answer:
Show hint
The AI learns patterns from examples. What pattern is in this data?
Show model answer
The AI will mostly recommend men, and reject good women applicants. Why: the AI has no idea what 'fair' means. It only finds patterns in the examples it was given. The pattern in this data is 'people who get hired here are usually men'. So it learns that pattern and repeats it. The AI is not being deliberately unfair - it is faithfully copying the unfairness that was already in the data. This is called BIAS and it is one of the most serious real problems with AI today. It has genuinely happened at real companies. The lesson: an AI is only as fair as the examples it learned from. Always ask - where did this data come from, and what unfairness might already be inside it?

WHAT HISTORY SHOWS: with printing, electricity and computers, some jobs disappeared, many changed, and new ones appeared that nobody had imagined. Calculators did not end mathematics. Written worksheet — 11 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
What is the main difference between a normal program and AI?
Show answer
In a normal program the human writes the rules. In AI the computer works out the rules itself by looking at many examples.
2
Why would writing rules to tell cats from dogs in photos fail?

3
What is machine learning?
Show answer
The method where you show a computer many labelled examples and it finds the patterns itself, instead of you writing the rules.
4
Write the four steps of how an AI learns.
Show answer
1. Guess (randomly at first) 2. Check against the correct answer 3. Measure how wrong it was (the loss) 4. Adjust slightly to be less wrong Then repeat millions of times.
5
What does a chatbot like ChatGPT actually do?
Show answer
It predicts what word should come next, over and over, one word at a time. It is not looking up facts.
6
What is hallucination and why can it not be fully fixed?
Show answer
When an AI states something false very confidently. It cannot be fully fixed because the AI predicts likely-sounding text rather than checking facts - it has no way to tell a true statement from a false one that sounds equally likely.
7
List four things AI cannot do.
Show answer
1. Understand anything - it only finds patterns in numbers 2. Feel emotions 3. Think about things it never saw in training 4. Want anything - it has no goals or consciousness
8
What is bias in AI? Give an example.
Show answer
When an AI copies unfairness that was already in its training examples. Example: a hiring AI trained on 20 years of decisions that mostly hired men will learn to recommend men and reject good women applicants. It is not being deliberately unfair - it is faithfully repeating the pattern it was shown.
9
Name four places you meet AI in everyday life.
Show answer
YouTube recommendations, voice typing, Google Photos face grouping, spam filters, Google Maps traffic, chatbots.
10
Give a balanced answer to: will AI take all the jobs?
Show answer
AI is good at repetitive tasks with clear patterns, and those jobs are genuinely changing. It is poor at judgement, understanding people, physical skill and responsibility. With past technologies - printing, electricity, computers - some jobs went, many changed, and new ones appeared. Calculators did not end mathematics. Honestly, nobody knows the exact scale of the change. But understanding how AI works puts you in a far better position than ignoring it.
11
Now that you have finished this track, what are three good next steps?
Show answer
1. Build small real programs - a marks calculator, a quiz game, a shopping list 2. Move on to the main tracks on this site for Python in depth, then databases, then machine learning 3. Take school maths seriously, especially averages, percentages, graphs and probability - AI is built on these
STAGE 2

📘 Getting Fluent

You can write basic programs. Now handle text properly, structure real data, and write Python the way working programmers write it.

2 tracks · 5 lessons · 40 exercises

📝 Working with Text

You know variables, loops and lists. Now learn to handle text properly — the skill behind every form, every file, every message your programs will ever process.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Cut, join, search and clean text confidently
  • Format numbers and text for neat output
  • Handle messy real-world text safely
  • Know why text bugs happen and how to prevent them
Lesson 10

Text is a sequence you can cut

📝 Working with Text · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

Where we are

In the school track you used text in simple ways — storing a name, printing a message. Now we go properly into it, because most real programming is text handling. Names, addresses, dates, file contents, messages, web pages — all text.

A string is a row of characters, each with a position

Remember how lists had positions starting at 0? Text works exactly the same way.

word = "PYTHON"
        012345    <-- position of each letter

word[0]    gives  'P'
word[3]    gives  'H'
word[-1]   gives  'N'    (last one, counting backwards)

This is the same idea as marks[0] from the lists lesson. Text is just a list of letters that Python treats specially.

Slicing: taking a piece out

word = "PYTHON"

word[0:3]    gives  'PYT'      from position 0, stop BEFORE 3
word[2:5]    gives  'THO'
word[:3]     gives  'PYT'      no start given means "from the beginning"
word[3:]     gives  'HON'      no end given means "to the end"
word[:]      gives  'PYTHON'   the whole thing

The rule is always the same: start is included, end is not. Same as range(0, 3) giving 0, 1, 2. Python is consistent about this everywhere, which is why it is worth learning once properly.

Worked example 1 — pulling apart a roll number

School roll numbers often encode information. Say the format is 2026CS042 — year, department, student number.

roll = "2026CS042"

year = roll[0:4]        '2026'
dept = roll[4:6]        'CS'
num  = roll[6:]         '042'

print(year)
print(dept)
print(num)

Let us count the positions carefully to be sure:

2 0 2 6 C S 0 4 2
0 1 2 3 4 5 6 7 8
  • roll[0:4] takes positions 0, 1, 2, 3 — that is 2, 0, 2, 6. Correct.
  • roll[4:6] takes positions 4 and 5 — C and S. Correct.
  • roll[6:] takes position 6 to the end — 0, 4, 2. Correct.

Output:

2026
CS
042
Notice something important. num holds the text "042", not the number 42. If you need to do maths with it, you must convert: int(num) gives 42. This is the same trap as the input lesson in the school track.

Length and searching

text = "hello world"

len(text)              11     how many characters (the space counts)
"world" in text        True   is this piece present anywhere?
text.find("world")     6      at which position does it start?
text.find("xyz")       -1     not found returns -1, it does not crash
text.count("l")        3      how many times does l appear?

Worked example 2 — checking an email looks reasonable

email = "ravi@school.edu"

has_at  = "@" in email
has_dot = "." in email

if has_at and has_dot:
    print("Looks like an email")
else:
    print("Not a valid email")

Output: Looks like an email

This is not a complete check — a@b.c would also pass, and real email validation is genuinely complicated. But it catches the common mistakes, and knowing the limits of your own check is part of being a good programmer.

Strings cannot be changed

This surprises people who have got comfortable with lists.

marks = [10, 20, 30]
marks[0] = 99          works fine, lists can be changed

word = "hello"
word[0] = "H"          ERROR - strings cannot be changed

Instead you build a new string:

word = "hello"
word = "H" + word[1:]     'H' joined to 'ello'
print(word)               Hello

The old string is thrown away and the box now holds a brand new one. Programmers say strings are immutable — unchangeable.

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

In the word PYTHON, what position is the letter T at?
Show answer

2
What does word[0:3] give for the word PYTHON, and why?
Show answer
PYT. The slice takes positions 0, 1 and 2. The start is included but the end is NOT.
3
What is the difference between word[:3] and word[3:]?
Show answer
word[:3] means from the beginning up to (not including) position 3. word[3:] means from position 3 to the very end.
4
What does word[-1] give and why is it useful?
Show answer
The last character. Negative positions count backwards from the end. Useful because you do not need to know the length first.
5

Show answer
-1. It does not crash. This lets you check whether something was found.

Show answer
Because strings are immutable - they cannot be changed after they are made. You must build a new string instead: word = 'H' + word[1:]

Show answer
Because it is still text, not a number. You must convert it first with int(num), which gives 42.

Lesson 11

Cleaning and reshaping text

📝 Working with Text · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

Real data is messy

When people type things in, they add extra spaces, use random capitals, and make small mistakes. If your program does not handle that, it breaks in ways that look mysterious.

Here is the classic bug. A student types their city as " Chennai " with spaces, and your check fails:

city = " Chennai "
if city == "Chennai":
    print("Found")     this NEVER runs

The two strings look identical to a human. To the computer they are different — one has spaces. This exact bug wastes hours of people's time every day, everywhere.

The cleaning tools

text = "  Hello World  "

text.strip()      'Hello World'    removes spaces from both ends
text.lstrip()     'Hello World  '  left side only
text.rstrip()     '  Hello World'  right side only
text.lower()      '  hello world  '   everything lowercase
text.upper()      '  HELLO WORLD  '   everything uppercase
text.title()      '  Hello World  '   First Letter Of Each Word
Critical detail: none of these change the original. They return a new string. This is because strings are immutable, which you learned last lesson. 
text.strip()          does nothing useful - the result is thrown away
text = text.strip()   correct - you keep the result
Forgetting to reassign is one of the most common beginner mistakes with strings.

Worked example 1 — the safe comparison pattern

city = "  cHeNnAi  "
answer = city.strip().lower()

if answer == "chennai":
    print("Match found")
else:
    print("No match")

Reading city.strip().lower() from left to right:

  • Start with " cHeNnAi "
  • .strip() removes the outer spaces, giving "cHeNnAi"
  • .lower() is then applied to that result, giving "chennai"

Output: Match found

Chaining methods like this — one after another with dots — is normal Python. Each one hands its result to the next.

Make this a habit: always clean text before comparing it. .strip().lower() should be automatic whenever you handle anything a human typed.

Splitting text apart

sentence = "python is really useful"
words = sentence.split()
print(words)

Output: ['python', 'is', 'really', 'useful']

split() gives you a list — the same lists you learned in the school track. So everything you know about lists now applies to text.

print(len(words))       4      how many words
print(words[0])         python  the first word

Worked example 2 — splitting on something specific

Data files often separate values with commas:

row = "Asha,15,Chennai,88"
parts = row.split(",")

print(parts)
print(parts[0])
print(int(parts[3]) + 2)

Output:

['Asha', '15', 'Chennai', '88']
Asha
90

Notice again — parts[3] is the text "88", so it needs int() before doing maths. Every value that comes from splitting text is text.

This is exactly how spreadsheet and CSV files are read. You now understand the core of it.

Joining text back together

words = ["python", "is", "useful"]

" ".join(words)      'python is useful'      joined with spaces
"-".join(words)      'python-is-useful'      joined with dashes
"".join(words)       'pythonisuseful'        joined with nothing

The syntax looks backwards at first. Read it as: "take this separator, and use it to join that list".

Worked example 3 — cleaning a messy list of names

raw = "  asha , RAVI ,meena  "

names = raw.split(",")
clean = []

for n in names:
    clean.append(n.strip().title())

print(clean)

Tracing it round by round:

After split:   ['  asha ', ' RAVI ', 'meena  ']
Round 1: '  asha '  -> strip -> 'asha'  -> title -> 'Asha'
Round 2: ' RAVI '   -> strip -> 'RAVI'  -> title -> 'Ravi'
Round 3: 'meena  '  -> strip -> 'meena' -> title -> 'Meena'

Output: ['Asha', 'Ravi', 'Meena']

You have just written a genuine data-cleaning program. This is real work that real jobs pay for.

Replacing parts

text = "I like tea"
text.replace("tea", "coffee")     'I like coffee'

phone = "98765-43210"
phone.replace("-", "")            '9876543210'

Again — this returns a new string. Reassign it if you want to keep the change.

What does .strip() do?
Show answer
Removes spaces (and other whitespace) from both ends of the string.

What is wrong with writing text.strip() on its own line?
Show answer
Nothing happens usefully. Strings are immutable, so .strip() returns a NEW string. If you do not store it with text = text.strip(), the result is thrown away.

Lesson 12

Formatting output that looks professional

📝 Working with Text · 25 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

Why this matters more than you think

Two programs can calculate exactly the same answer. One shows:

Average: 78.33333333333333

The other shows:

Average: 78.3%

The second one looks like a finished product. The first looks unfinished. Same maths, different impression — and impressions matter when someone is deciding whether your work is any good.

Recap: f-strings

You met these in the school track. The f before the quote lets you put variables inside curly brackets:

name = "Asha"
marks = 88
print(f"{name} scored {marks}")     Asha scored 88

Controlling decimal places

Put a colon and a format code inside the brackets:

value = 78.33333333333333

f"{value:.0f}"     '78'        no decimal places
f"{value:.1f}"     '78.3'      one decimal place
f"{value:.2f}"     '78.33'     two decimal places

Read :.2f as "format as a number with 2 digits after the point". The f here means "fixed point", not the f-string f. Slightly confusing, but you get used to it.

Worked example 1 — a clean marks report

marks = [78, 92, 65, 88, 71]
average = sum(marks) / len(marks)

print(f"Students: {len(marks)}")
print(f"Total:    {sum(marks)}")
print(f"Average:  {average:.1f}")
print(f"Highest:  {max(marks)}")

Output:

Students: 5
Total:    394
Average:  78.8
Highest:  92

Without :.1f the average would print as 78.8 here by luck, but with other numbers you would get 78.33333333333333. Always format numbers you show to people.

Lining columns up

Put a number after the colon to reserve a fixed width:

f"{name:10}"     text padded to 10 characters, left aligned
f"{marks:>5}"    padded to 5, pushed RIGHT
f"{name:<10}"    padded to 10, pushed LEFT (same as default for text)
f"{title:^20}"   centred in 20 characters

Worked example 2 — a neat table

students = [("Asha", 88), ("Ravichandran", 65), ("Meena", 92)]

print(f"{'NAME':<15}{'MARKS':>6}")
print("-" * 21)

for name, marks in students:
    print(f"{name:<15}{marks:>6}")

Output:

NAME            MARKS
---------------------
Asha               88
Ravichandran       65
Meena              92

Two new things here worth noticing:

  • "-" * 21 — multiplying text repeats it. Handy for drawing lines.
  • for name, marks in students — each item is a pair, and Python unpacks it into two variables at once. This is called tuple unpacking, and it saves you writing student[0] and student[1] everywhere.

Numbers are pushed right (>) because that is how humans read numeric columns — the units line up underneath each other.

Percentages and thousands separators

rate = 0.8756
f"{rate:.1%}"        '87.6%'      turns it into a percentage automatically

big = 1234567
f"{big:,}"           '1,234,567'  adds commas

The % format multiplies by 100 and adds the sign for you. Do not multiply by 100 yourself and then add % — you will end up doing it twice one day.

Worked example 3 — a bill

items = [("Notebook", 3, 45.50), ("Pen", 5, 12.00)]
total = 0

print(f"{'ITEM':<12}{'QTY':>4}{'RATE':>9}{'AMOUNT':>10}")
print("=" * 35)

for name, qty, rate in items:
    amount = qty * rate
    total = total + amount
    print(f"{name:<12}{qty:>4}{rate:>9.2f}{amount:>10.2f}")

print("=" * 35)
print(f"{'TOTAL':<12}{'':>4}{'':>9}{total:>10.2f}")

Output:

ITEM         QTY     RATE    AMOUNT
===================================
Notebook       3    45.50     136.50
Pen            5    12.00      60.00
===================================
TOTAL                          196.50

Notice {rate:>9.2f} combines both ideas — width 9, and 2 decimal places. You can stack format instructions like this.

A habit worth forming now: whenever you show a number to a human, ask yourself how many decimal places actually make sense. Money always gets 2. Percentages usually get 1. Counts get 0. Showing 78.33333333333333 tells the reader you did not think about it.

Practice — 8 exercises

    # f"{name:<12}{marks:>5}"
    pass

Challenge: print a small bill. For each item show name, quantity and the amount (qty times rate) to 2 decimals. Then show the total.
items = [("Notebook", 3, 45.50), ("Pen", 5, 12.00)]

for name, qty, rate in items:
    # calculate, add to total, print
    pass

Show hint
amount = qty * rate; total = total + amount; print(f"{name} {qty} {amount:.2f}"). Total is 196.50
Show solution
items = [("Notebook", 3, 45.50), ("Pen", 5, 12.00)]
total = 0
for name, qty, rate in items:
    amount = qty * rate
    total = total + amount
    print(f"{name} {qty} {amount:.2f}")
print(f"TOTAL {total:.2f}")

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Why does formatting output matter if the calculation is already correct?
Show answer
Because unformatted output like 78.33333333333333 looks unfinished. The same answer shown as 78.3% looks like a finished product. It affects whether people trust your work.
2
What does :.2f do?
Show answer
Formats the number with exactly 2 digits after the decimal point.
3
How many decimal places should money have? Percentages? Counts?
Show answer
Money: always 2. Percentages: usually 1. Counts: 0 (they are whole numbers).
4
What does :>5 do and when would you use it?
Show answer
Pads to 5 characters and pushes the content right. Used for numbers in a table so the units line up underneath each other.
5
What does :<12 do?
Show answer
Pads to 12 characters, pushed left. Used for names and text in a table.
6
What does 'x' * 20 produce and why is it useful?
Show answer
Twenty x characters in a row. Multiplying text repeats it. Useful for drawing separator lines in reports.
7
What does :.1% do to the value 0.8756?
Show answer
Gives 87.6%. It multiplies by 100 and adds the percent sign automatically.
8
Why should you NOT multiply by 100 yourself before using the % format?
Show answer
Because the % format already multiplies by 100. Doing both gives 8756.0% instead of 87.6%.
9
Explain what happens in: for name, marks in students:
Show answer
Each item in the list is a pair. Python unpacks it into two variables at once. This is called tuple unpacking, and it avoids writing student[0] and student[1].
10
Write an f-string that shows a price right-aligned in 10 characters with 2 decimals.
Show answer
f"{price:>10.2f}"

🗂️ Organising Data

Lists hold values in a row. But real information has labels — a student has a name AND marks AND a city. Dictionaries let you store that properly, and they are the single most useful structure in Python.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Store labelled information with dictionaries
  • Choose correctly between a list and a dictionary
  • Handle collections of records the way real programs do
  • Write comprehensions to transform data in one line
Lesson 13

Dictionaries: values with labels

🗂️ Organising Data · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

The problem with lists

Say you store a student as a list:

student = ["Asha", 15, "Chennai", 88]

Now, three weeks later, you read student[2]. What is it? You have to count positions to find out. And if someone inserts a new field at the start, every number in your program is silently wrong.

A dictionary labels each value

student = {
    "name": "Asha",
    "age": 15,
    "city": "Chennai",
    "marks": 88
}

Now you write student["city"]. It says exactly what it is. Order does not matter. Adding new fields breaks nothing.

The structure is key: value pairs, in curly brackets, separated by commas. The key is the label; the value is what it holds.

Reading and writing

student["name"]              'Asha'
student["marks"] = 92        change an existing value
student["email"] = "a@b.c"   add a completely new one
del student["age"]           remove one

The safe way to read

student["phone"]                    CRASHES if phone is not there
student.get("phone")                gives None instead of crashing
student.get("phone", "not given")   gives your own default instead
Use .get() whenever a key might be missing. Real data has gaps — someone did not fill in a field, a record is incomplete. Using square brackets on missing data is one of the most common causes of programs crashing in production. .get() with a sensible default is almost always the right choice.

Worked example 1 — a student record

student = {"name": "Asha", "marks": 88, "city": "Chennai"}

print(student["name"])
print(student.get("phone", "not provided"))

student["marks"] = student["marks"] + 5
print(student["marks"])

Output:

Asha
not provided
93

Looping through a dictionary

student = {"name": "Asha", "marks": 88, "city": "Chennai"}

for key in student:
    print(key)                 just the labels

for key, value in student.items():
    print(f"{key}: {value}")   both at once

The second form is what you want almost always. Output:

name: Asha
marks: 88
city: Chennai

A list of dictionaries — the shape of real data

This combination is everywhere. Every database table, every spreadsheet, every API response looks like this:

students = [
    {"name": "Asha",  "marks": 88, "city": "Chennai"},
    {"name": "Ravi",  "marks": 65, "city": "Pune"},
    {"name": "Meena", "marks": 92, "city": "Chennai"},
]

A list holds the records. Each record is a dictionary with labelled fields.

Worked example 2 — processing records

for s in students:
    print(f"{s['name']:<8} {s['marks']:>3}")

Output:

Asha      88
Ravi      65
Meena     92

Note the quote marks: the f-string uses double quotes on the outside, so inside the curly brackets you use single quotes — s['name']. Mixing them up is a common small error.

Worked example 3 — answering real questions

total = 0
best = students[0]
chennai_count = 0

for s in students:
    total = total + s["marks"]
    if s["marks"] > best["marks"]:
        best = s
    if s["city"] == "Chennai":
        chennai_count = chennai_count + 1

print(f"Average: {total / len(students):.1f}")
print(f"Top: {best['name']} with {best['marks']}")
print(f"From Chennai: {chennai_count}")

Output:

Average: 81.7
Top: Meena with 92
From Chennai: 2

Look at what you just did. You calculated an average, found a maximum, and counted matching records — in one pass through the data. That is genuinely what data analysis is. The tools get fancier later, but the thinking is exactly this.

Counting things with a dictionary

This pattern comes up constantly, so learn it as a shape:

cities = ["Chennai", "Pune", "Chennai", "Delhi", "Pune", "Chennai"]
counts = {}

for city in cities:
    if city in counts:
        counts[city] = counts[city] + 1
    else:
        counts[city] = 1

print(counts)

Output: {'Chennai': 3, 'Pune': 2, 'Delhi': 1}

There is a shorter way using the default trick:

for city in cities:
    counts[city] = counts.get(city, 0) + 1

Read it as: take the current count (or 0 if we have not seen this city before), add one, store it back. One line instead of four.
