Lesson 14

Comprehensions: transforming data in one line

🗂️ Organising Data · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

A pattern you have written many times

Look at these three loops from earlier lessons:

squares = []
for i in range(1, 6):
    squares.append(i * i)

names = []
for s in students:
    names.append(s["name"])

passed = []
for m in marks:
    if m >= 40:
        passed.append(m)

They all have the same shape: make an empty list, loop, append. Python gives you a shortcut for exactly this shape.

The list comprehension

squares = [i * i for i in range(1, 6)]
print(squares)

Output: [1, 4, 9, 16, 25]

Read it in this order:

  • Start at the middle: for i in range(1, 6) — this is the loop, same as always
  • Then look left: i * i — this is what goes into the list each time
  • The square brackets around it mean the result is a list

It is the same loop, written on one line, with the append implied.

Worked example 1 — side by side

Long way:                      Short way:

names = []                     names = [s["name"] for s in students]
for s in students:
    names.append(s["name"])

Both give exactly the same result. The short way is not clever showing-off — it is the normal way Python programmers write this, and you will see it constantly in real code.

Adding a condition

marks = [78, 35, 92, 40, 65]

passed = [m for m in marks if m >= 40]
print(passed)

Output: [78, 92, 40, 65]

The if goes at the end and filters — only items where the condition is true get included.

Now read the whole thing: take m, for each m in marks, if m is 40 or more. It reads almost like English.

Worked example 2 — transform and filter together

students = [
    {"name": "asha",  "marks": 88},
    {"name": "ravi",  "marks": 35},
    {"name": "meena", "marks": 92},
]

toppers = [s["name"].title() for s in students if s["marks"] >= 80]
print(toppers)

Output: ['Asha', 'Meena']

Breaking it down:

  • for s in students — go through each record
  • if s["marks"] >= 80 — keep only the high scorers
  • s["name"].title() — for those kept, take the name and capitalise it

Ravi is skipped because 35 is below 80. The two who remain get their names tidied.

When NOT to use a comprehension

This is important, because beginners discover comprehensions and then cram everything into them.

result = [x*2 if x > 0 else x*3 for y in data for x in y if x != 0]

That is technically valid Python and completely unreadable. Nobody, including you next month, will understand it.

The rule: if the comprehension does not fit comfortably on one line, or if you have to read it twice, write the ordinary loop instead. Clear code that anyone can follow beats clever code every single time. Experienced programmers write more ordinary loops than beginners expect.

Dictionary comprehensions

The same idea builds dictionaries. Use curly brackets and a key: value pair:

students = [
    {"name": "Asha",  "marks": 88},
    {"name": "Ravi",  "marks": 65},
]

lookup = {s["name"]: s["marks"] for s in students}
print(lookup)

Output: {'Asha': 88, 'Ravi': 65}

Why is this useful? Because now looking up any student's marks is instant:

print(lookup["Asha"])      88

Without it you would loop through the whole list every time you wanted one student. With 10 students that does not matter. With 10 million it matters enormously — and understanding that difference is what the Big-O lesson later is about.

Worked example 3 — a small data pipeline

raw = ["  asha , 88 ", " ravi , 35", "meena,92 "]

records = []
for line in raw:
    parts = line.split(",")
    records.append({
        "name": parts[0].strip().title(),
        "marks": int(parts[1].strip())
    })

toppers = [r["name"] for r in records if r["marks"] >= 80]

print(records)
print(toppers)

Output:

[{'name': 'Asha', 'marks': 88}, {'name': 'Ravi', 'marks': 35}, {'name': 'Meena', 'marks': 92}]
['Asha', 'Meena']

Look at what this does: takes messy text, cleans it, converts types, builds structured records, then filters them. That is a data pipeline, and it is the single most common thing a working data analyst writes. You have all the pieces now.

Practice — 8 exercises

STAGE 3

🗄️ Databases & SQL

The highest-paying skill for the least effort. Every query here runs for real against a live database. SQL alone qualifies you for data analyst work.

1 tracks · 5 lessons · 40 exercises

🗄️ SQL & Databases

The highest-paying skill you can learn for the least effort. SQL alone qualifies you for data analyst jobs, and every application you will ever build sits on a database. Every exercise here runs real SQL.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Write SELECT queries with filtering and sorting
  • Summarise data with COUNT, SUM, AVG and GROUP BY
  • Combine information from multiple tables with JOIN
  • Design sensible tables with proper keys
  • Write queries that are safe from SQL injection
Lesson 15

What a database is, and asking it questions

🗄️ SQL & Databases · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 11 worksheet questions

Where we are, and why this matters

In Stage 2 you stored records as a list of dictionaries:

students = [
    {"name": "Asha", "marks": 88, "city": "Chennai"},
    {"name": "Ravi", "marks": 65, "city": "Pune"},
]

That works perfectly — for a few hundred records, that exist only while your program is running, and that only one person uses.

Now consider a real situation. A school with 4,000 students. The data must survive the computer being switched off. Twenty teachers need to read it at the same time. Two of them might edit the same record at the same instant. You need to find "all students in Chennai scoring above 75" in under a second.

A Python list cannot do any of that. A database can. That is the entire reason databases exist.

A table is a list of dictionaries, made permanent

Look at how similar these are:

Python                              Database
------------------------------      ------------------------------
a list                         =    a TABLE
one dictionary in the list     =    one ROW  (also called a record)
a key in the dictionary        =    a COLUMN (also called a field)
the value                      =    the value in that cell

The students table looks exactly like a spreadsheet:

id | name    | marks | city
---+---------+-------+---------
 1 | Asha    |    88 | Chennai
 2 | Ravi    |    65 | Pune
 3 | Meena   |    92 | Chennai
 4 | Karthik |    45 | Delhi
 5 | Divya   |    78 | Pune

One important difference from a Python list: every row must have exactly the same columns. You cannot give Ravi an extra field that Asha does not have. This strictness is a feature — it means the database can check your data and search it extremely fast.

SQL is how you ask questions

SQL stands for Structured Query Language. It is not a general programming language like Python — you cannot write a game in it. It does one job: getting information in and out of tables. And it has barely changed in fifty years, so what you learn now will still be true for your whole career.

The remarkable thing about SQL is that you describe what you want, not how to get it. In Python you would write a loop. In SQL you just say what you are looking for and the database works out the fastest way to find it.

Your first query

SELECT name FROM students;

Read it as plain English: select the name column, from the students table.

Result:

Asha
Ravi
Meena
Karthik
Divya

Two rules of style, worth adopting from your very first query:

  • SQL keywords in CAPITALS — SELECT, FROM, WHERE. Your own names stay lowercase. SQL does not require this, but every workplace does it, because it makes queries scannable.
  • A semicolon at the end. It marks where the statement finishes.

Choosing columns

SELECT name FROM students;               one column
SELECT name, marks FROM students;        two columns, in that order
SELECT * FROM students;                  the star means EVERY column
A habit worth forming now: use SELECT * when you are exploring and want to see what is in a table. But in real code, always name the columns you actually need. If someone later adds fifteen columns to that table, SELECT * silently starts dragging all of them across the network on every single query.

Worked example 1 — selecting specific columns

SELECT name, city FROM students;

Result:

Asha Chennai
Ravi Pune
Meena Chennai
Karthik Delhi
Divya Pune

Notice the database gave back the columns in the order you asked for them, and left marks and id out entirely. You get exactly what you requested, nothing more.

WHERE — keeping only the rows you want

This is where SQL becomes genuinely useful.

SELECT name, marks
FROM students
WHERE marks > 75;

Result:

Asha 88
Meena 92
Divya 78

The database went through every row, kept only those where marks was above 75, and returned the columns you asked for. In Python that would be a loop with an if inside. Here it is one line, and it stays one line whether the table has 5 rows or 50 million.

The comparison operators

marks = 88        equal to        (ONE equals sign, not two!)
marks != 88       not equal to
marks > 75        greater than
marks < 50        less than
marks >= 75       greater than or equal
marks <= 40       less than or equal
Careful — this trips up every Python programmer. In Python you use == to compare. In SQL you use a single =. SQL has no assignment operator inside a query, so there is no ambiguity to worry about. Just remember: Python ==, SQL =.

Comparing text

WHERE city = 'Chennai'

Text goes in single quotes in SQL. Not double quotes — those mean something different (they refer to column names). Single quotes for text values, always.

Worked example 2 — filtering on text

SELECT name, marks
FROM students
WHERE city = 'Chennai';

Result:

Asha 88
Meena 92

Combining conditions with AND and OR

WHERE marks > 75 AND city = 'Chennai'     both must be true
WHERE city = 'Pune' OR city = 'Delhi'     either one is enough
WHERE NOT city = 'Chennai'                flips it around

These are the same and / or / not ideas you learned in Stage 1, just written in capitals.

Worked example 3 — two conditions at once

SELECT name, marks, city
FROM students
WHERE marks > 75 AND city = 'Chennai';

Let us trace it row by row, exactly as the database does:

Asha    88 Chennai  ->  88>75 yes, Chennai yes  ->  KEEP
Ravi    65 Pune     ->  65>75 NO                ->  skip
Meena   92 Chennai  ->  92>75 yes, Chennai yes  ->  KEEP
Karthik 45 Delhi    ->  45>75 NO                ->  skip
Divya   78 Pune     ->  78>75 yes, Chennai NO   ->  skip

Result:

Asha 88 Chennai
Meena 92 Chennai

Three more filters you will use constantly

WHERE city IN ('Pune', 'Delhi')          matches any in the list
WHERE marks BETWEEN 60 AND 80            inclusive at BOTH ends
WHERE name LIKE 'A%'                     starts with A
WHERE name LIKE '%a'                     ends with a
WHERE name LIKE '%ee%'                   contains 'ee' anywhere

In LIKE, the % symbol means "any number of any characters". It is SQL's wildcard.

IN is much nicer than writing city = 'Pune' OR city = 'Delhi' OR city = 'Mumbai' — same meaning, far more readable.

Watch out for BETWEEN: it includes both ends. BETWEEN 60 AND 80 includes a student with exactly 60 and one with exactly 80. This differs from Python's range(), which excludes the end — so do not carry that assumption across.

Running SQL from Python

Every exercise here uses this pattern. Python has a small database called SQLite built into it, so nothing needs installing:

import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")      a temporary database in RAM

query = """
SELECT name FROM students
"""

for row in db.execute(query):
    print(*row)

Two small Python details worth understanding:

  • Triple quotes """ let a string run across several lines. SQL queries are much easier to read spread over multiple lines, so this is the normal way to write them.
  • The star in print(*row) unpacks the row. Each row comes back as a tuple like ('Asha', 88). Without the star you would see the brackets and quotes; with it you see Asha 88.

Written worksheet — 11 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Why can a Python list of dictionaries not replace a database for a real school with 4,000 students?
Show answer
The data disappears when the program stops. Many people cannot read and write it at the same time. There is no protection when two people edit the same record at once. And searching gets slow as it grows.
2
Match the Python idea to the database word: a list, one dictionary, a key.
Show answer
a list = a TABLE one dictionary = a ROW (or record) a key = a COLUMN (or field)
3
What is one important way a table is stricter than a Python list of dictionaries?
Show answer
Every row must have exactly the same columns. You cannot give one row an extra field. This strictness lets the database check the data and search it very fast.
4
What does SQL stand for, and what is it for?
Show answer
Structured Query Language. It does one job: getting information in and out of tables. It is not a general programming language.
5
Write the query to select all columns from a table called orders.
Show answer
SELECT * FROM orders;
6
Why should you avoid SELECT * in real production code?
Show answer
If someone later adds fifteen columns to that table, your query silently starts pulling all of them across the network every time. Name the columns you actually need.
7
What is the single biggest difference between comparing in Python and comparing in SQL?
Show answer
Python uses == (two equals signs). SQL uses = (one equals sign).
8
Should text values use single or double quotes in SQL? Why does it matter?
Show answer
Single quotes. Double quotes mean something different in SQL - they refer to column names, not text values.
9
Write a query to find employees earning more than 50000 in the city Pune.
Show answer
SELECT name, salary FROM employees WHERE salary > 50000 AND city = 'Pune';
10
Explain BETWEEN 60 AND 80. How does it differ from Python's range(60, 80)?
Show answer
BETWEEN includes BOTH ends - a value of exactly 60 and exactly 80 both match. Python's range(60, 80) excludes the end, stopping at 79. Do not carry the Python assumption across.
11
What does the % symbol mean inside LIKE? Give an example.
Show answer
It is a wildcard meaning 'any number of any characters'. LIKE 'A%' means starts with A. LIKE '%a' means ends with a. LIKE '%ee%' means contains ee anywhere.
Lesson 16

Sorting, limiting, and summarising

🗄️ SQL & Databases · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 11 worksheet questions

ORDER BY — putting rows in order

Rows come back in whatever order the database finds convenient. If you want a specific order, you must ask for it.

SELECT name, marks
FROM students
ORDER BY marks;

Result — lowest first, because ascending is the default:

Karthik 45
Ravi 65
Divya 78
Asha 88
Meena 92

To flip it, add DESC (short for descending):

SELECT name, marks
FROM students
ORDER BY marks DESC;
Meena 92
Asha 88
Divya 78
Ravi 65
Karthik 45

You can also write ASC for ascending, but since it is the default, most people leave it out.

Sorting by more than one column

SELECT name, city, marks
FROM students
ORDER BY city, marks DESC;

This sorts by city first. Then, within each city, it sorts by marks highest-first. The second column only matters when the first one ties.

Karthik Delhi   45
Meena   Chennai 92     Chennai group, highest first
Asha    Chennai 88
Divya   Pune    78     Pune group, highest first
Ravi    Pune    65

(Cities sort alphabetically: Chennai, Delhi, Pune.)

LIMIT — only the first few

SELECT name, marks
FROM students
ORDER BY marks DESC
LIMIT 3;

Result — the top three:

Meena 92
Asha 88
Divya 78
ORDER BY and LIMIT belong together. LIMIT 3 on its own gives you three rows, but arbitrary ones — whichever the database happened to reach first. Only when you sort first does "LIMIT 3" mean something meaningful like "the top three".

DISTINCT — removing duplicates

SELECT city FROM students;              gives Chennai, Pune, Chennai, Delhi, Pune
SELECT DISTINCT city FROM students;     gives Chennai, Pune, Delhi

Useful whenever you want to know "what different values exist in this column?"

Aggregate functions — turning many rows into one number

Everything so far returned rows. These return a single value calculated from all the rows.

COUNT(*)        how many rows
SUM(marks)      add them all up
AVG(marks)      the average
MAX(marks)      the largest
MIN(marks)      the smallest

You already know these from Python — len(), sum(), max(), min(). Same ideas, different names.

Worked example 1 — counting

SELECT COUNT(*) FROM students;

Result: 5

One row, one number. The five rows were collapsed into a single answer.

Worked example 2 — counting with a filter

SELECT COUNT(*)
FROM students
WHERE marks >= 75;

Result: 3

The WHERE runs first, keeping Asha, Meena and Divya. Then COUNT(*) counts what survived. Filter first, then summarise — that order matters and it is always this way round.

Worked example 3 — several summaries at once

SELECT COUNT(*), AVG(marks), MAX(marks), MIN(marks)
FROM students;

Result:

5 73.6 92 45

One row containing four different summaries. The database read the table once and calculated all four as it went.

Naming your results with AS

A column called COUNT(*) is ugly and hard to work with. Rename it:

SELECT COUNT(*) AS total_students,
       AVG(marks) AS average_marks
FROM students;

The result is the same numbers, but the columns now have proper names. This is called an alias. It matters enormously once you start writing bigger queries or feeding results into other programs.

Rounding

AVG often gives long decimals. ROUND tidies it:

SELECT ROUND(AVG(marks), 1) AS average
FROM students;

Result: 73.6

The 1 is how many decimal places you want — the same thinking as :.1f in Python f-strings.

A subtle but important detail about COUNT

COUNT(*)          counts every row
COUNT(city)       counts rows where city is NOT empty

Databases have a special value called NULL, which means "no value here" — nobody filled it in. NULL is not zero and not an empty string; it is the absence of information.

COUNT(*) counts rows regardless. COUNT(column) skips NULLs. So comparing the two tells you how much data is missing:

SELECT COUNT(*), COUNT(phone) FROM students;
5  2       -- means three students have no phone number recorded
NULL behaves strangely and it catches people out. WHERE phone = NULL does not work — it returns nothing at all, even when there are NULLs. This is because NULL means "unknown", and asking whether an unknown equals an unknown gives... unknown. You must write WHERE phone IS NULL or WHERE phone IS NOT NULL. Remember IS, not =.

    (3, "Meena",   92, "Chennai"),
    (4, "Karthik", 45, "Delhi"),
    (5, "Divya",   78, "Pune"),
])

query = """
SELECT COUNT(*) FROM students
"""

for row in db.execute(query):
    print(*row)

Written worksheet — 11 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
What order do rows come back in if you do not use ORDER BY?
Show answer
Whatever order the database finds convenient. It is not guaranteed and can change. If you need a specific order, you must ask for it.
2
What is the default sort direction, and how do you reverse it?
Show answer
Ascending (smallest first) is the default. Add DESC to reverse it.
3
Explain ORDER BY city, marks DESC in your own words.
Show answer
Sort by city first. Then, within each city group, sort by marks highest first. The second column only matters when the first one ties.
4
Why is LIMIT 3 on its own almost always a mistake?
Show answer
Without ORDER BY you get three arbitrary rows - whichever the database reached first. Only after sorting does 'the first 3' mean something meaningful like 'the top 3'.
5
What does DISTINCT do?
Show answer
Removes duplicate values, so you see each different value only once.
6
Name the five aggregate functions and their Python equivalents.
Show answer
COUNT(*) = len() SUM() = sum() AVG() = sum()/len() MAX() = max() MIN() = min()
7
In a query with both WHERE and COUNT, which happens first?
Show answer
WHERE runs first, keeping only the matching rows. Then COUNT counts what survived. Filter first, summarise second - always.
8
What does AS do and why does it matter?
Show answer
It renames a result column (an alias). It matters because COUNT(*) is an ugly column name, and proper names are essential when queries get bigger or feed into other programs.
9
Write a query showing the average salary rounded to 2 decimal places, named avg_pay.
Show answer
SELECT ROUND(AVG(salary), 2) AS avg_pay FROM employees;
10
What is the difference between COUNT(*) and COUNT(phone)?
Show answer
COUNT(*) counts every row. COUNT(phone) counts only rows where phone is not NULL. Comparing the two tells you how much data is missing.
11
What is NULL, and why does WHERE phone = NULL fail?
Show answer
NULL means 'no value / unknown'. It is not zero and not an empty string. Comparing an unknown to an unknown gives unknown, not true - so nothing ever matches. You must write WHERE phone IS NULL (using IS, not =).
Lesson 17

GROUP BY — summarising by category

🗄️ SQL & Databases · 40 min read · 8 exercises · 11 worksheet questions

The question aggregates cannot answer yet

You can now find the average marks of the whole school:

SELECT AVG(marks) FROM students;      one number for everybody

But the real question a head teacher asks is different: "what is the average in each city?" Not one number — one number per city.

You could run three separate queries, one per city. But you would need to know the cities in advance, and it would break the moment a new city appeared.

GROUP BY does it in one query

SELECT city, AVG(marks)
FROM students
GROUP BY city;

Result:

Chennai 90.0
Delhi 45.0
Pune 71.5

What GROUP BY actually does — picture it

Imagine the database physically sorting the rows into piles, one pile per city:

PILE "Chennai"          PILE "Pune"            PILE "Delhi"
Asha    88              Ravi    65             Karthik 45
Meena   92              Divya   78

Then it applies your aggregate function to each pile separately, and gives you one row back per pile:

Chennai -> AVG(88, 92) = 90.0
Pune    -> AVG(65, 78) = 71.5
Delhi   -> AVG(45)     = 45.0

That mental picture — sort into piles, summarise each pile — is the whole of GROUP BY. Hold on to it and the rest follows.

Worked example 1 — counting per group

SELECT city, COUNT(*)
FROM students
GROUP BY city;

Result:

Chennai 2
Delhi 1
Pune 2

This is exactly the counting-dictionary pattern from Stage 2, where you wrote:

counts[city] = counts.get(city, 0) + 1

Four lines of Python, or one line of SQL. And the SQL version works just as fast on ten million rows.

Worked example 2 — several summaries per group

SELECT city,
       COUNT(*) AS students,
       ROUND(AVG(marks), 1) AS average,
       MAX(marks) AS best
FROM students
GROUP BY city
ORDER BY average DESC;

Result:

Chennai 2 90.0 92
Pune 2 71.5 78
Delhi 1 45.0 45

Read that result as a genuine report: Chennai has 2 students averaging 90, best mark 92. This is real data analysis, and you wrote it in six lines.

The rule that catches everybody

Every column in your SELECT must either be in the GROUP BY, or inside an aggregate function. Nothing else is allowed.

SELECT city, name, AVG(marks)      WRONG
FROM students
GROUP BY city;

Why is this wrong? Think about the piles. The Chennai pile contains Asha and Meena. You asked for one row per city, and one name value in it. Which name should the database put — Asha or Meena? There is no sensible answer, so the question itself is broken.

Whenever you get an error mentioning GROUP BY, go through your SELECT columns one at a time and ask of each: is this in the GROUP BY, or wrapped in COUNT/SUM/AVG/MAX/MIN? If it is neither, that column is your problem. This single check resolves almost every GROUP BY error you will ever hit.

HAVING — filtering the groups

Now a harder question: "which cities have an average above 70?"

Your instinct is to use WHERE. It does not work:

SELECT city, AVG(marks)
FROM students
WHERE AVG(marks) > 70        ERROR
GROUP BY city;

The reason is about timing. WHERE runs while the database is still looking at individual rows — before any piles have been made. At that moment no average exists yet. You cannot filter on something that has not been calculated.

HAVING is the filter that runs after grouping:

SELECT city, AVG(marks)
FROM students
GROUP BY city
HAVING AVG(marks) > 70;

Result:

Chennai 90.0
Pune 71.5

Delhi is gone — its average of 45 did not survive the HAVING.

The order the database does things

This is worth memorising. It explains most SQL confusion:

1. FROM       which table
2. WHERE      throw away individual rows
3. GROUP BY   sort what remains into piles
4. HAVING     throw away whole piles
5. SELECT     work out the columns to show
6. ORDER BY   sort the final result
7. LIMIT      keep only the first few

Notice that SELECT — which you write first — actually happens fifth. That is why you cannot use a column alias in WHERE: at that point in the process, the alias does not exist yet.

Worked example 3 — WHERE and HAVING together

They are not alternatives. Serious queries often use both:

SELECT city, COUNT(*) AS passed
FROM students
WHERE marks >= 60
GROUP BY city
HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2;

Trace it step by step:

Step 2 - WHERE marks >= 60 keeps:
   Asha 88 Chennai, Ravi 65 Pune, Meena 92 Chennai, Divya 78 Pune
   (Karthik with 45 is thrown out here)

Step 3 - GROUP BY city makes two piles:
   Chennai: Asha, Meena   ->  count 2
   Pune:    Ravi, Divya   ->  count 2

Step 4 - HAVING COUNT(*) >= 2 keeps both piles

Result:

Chennai 2
Pune 2

Read the query as one sentence: of the students who passed, show me the cities that have at least two of them.

The one-line summary that will get you through interviews: WHERE filters rows before grouping. HAVING filters groups after grouping. This is asked constantly and most candidates fumble it.

Written worksheet — 11 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Describe what GROUP BY does using the 'piles' picture.
Show answer
It sorts the rows into piles, one pile per distinct value. Then it applies your aggregate function to each pile separately, giving one result row per pile.
2
Write a query showing how many employees work in each department.
Show answer
SELECT department, COUNT(*) FROM employees GROUP BY department;
3
Why is this query wrong? SELECT city, name, AVG(marks) FROM students GROUP BY city;
Show answer
Because the Chennai pile contains two different names. You asked for one row per city with one name in it, but there is no way to choose between Asha and Meena. Every SELECT column must be either in the GROUP BY or inside an aggregate function.
4
What check should you run when you get a GROUP BY error?
Show answer
Go through each SELECT column and ask: is it in the GROUP BY, or wrapped in COUNT/SUM/AVG/MAX/MIN? If neither, that is the problem column.
5
Why can you not write WHERE AVG(marks) > 70?
Show answer
Because WHERE runs while the database is still looking at individual rows, before any groups have been formed. At that moment no average exists yet.
6
What is HAVING and when do you use it?
Show answer
A filter that runs AFTER grouping. Use it to throw away whole groups based on an aggregate value.
7
Write out the order in which a database actually processes a query.
Show answer
1. FROM - which table 2. WHERE - throw away rows 3. GROUP BY - sort into piles 4. HAVING - throw away piles 5. SELECT - work out columns 6. ORDER BY - sort the result 7. LIMIT - keep the first few
8
Why can you use a column alias in ORDER BY but not in WHERE?
Show answer
Because SELECT (where the alias is created) happens at step 5. WHERE is step 2, so the alias does not exist yet. ORDER BY is step 6, which is after SELECT, so it does.
9
Give the one-line summary of WHERE vs HAVING.
Show answer
WHERE filters rows before grouping. HAVING filters groups after grouping.
10
Write a query: among orders over 500, show customers who placed at least 3 such orders.
Show answer
SELECT customer_id, COUNT(*) AS n FROM orders WHERE amount > 500 GROUP BY customer_id HAVING COUNT(*) >= 3;
11
How does GROUP BY relate to the counting-dictionary pattern from Stage 2?
Show answer
They do the same job. In Python you wrote counts[city] = counts.get(city, 0) + 1 in a loop - four lines. In SQL it is GROUP BY city with COUNT(*) - one line, and it stays fast on millions of rows.
Lesson 18

JOIN — combining two tables

🗄️ SQL & Databases · 40 min read · 8 exercises · 11 worksheet questions

Why data gets split across tables

Suppose each student has a class teacher. The lazy approach puts everything in one table:

id | name  | marks | city    | teacher_name | teacher_subject
 1 | Asha  |    88 | Chennai | Mrs Rao      | Maths
 2 | Ravi  |    65 | Pune    | Mr Iyer      | Science
 3 | Meena |    92 | Chennai | Mrs Rao      | Maths

Look at the problem. "Mrs Rao" and "Maths" are written twice. With 200 students she might appear 40 times.

Now Mrs Rao changes surname. You must update 40 rows. Miss one and your data is permanently inconsistent — the same person recorded under two names, and no way to tell which is right.

The fix: separate tables, connected by an id

TABLE teachers                    TABLE students
id | name     | subject           id | name  | marks | teacher_id
 1 | Mrs Rao  | Maths              1 | Asha  |    88 |          1
 2 | Mr Iyer  | Science            2 | Ravi  |    65 |          2
 3 | Ms Nair  | English            3 | Meena |    92 |          1

Mrs Rao's details now exist in exactly one place. Students point at her using her id number. Change her name once and every student automatically reflects it.

Two pieces of vocabulary:

  • Primary key — the column that uniquely identifies a row. teachers.id. Every table should have one.
  • Foreign key — a column pointing at another table's primary key. students.teacher_id. The word "foreign" just means "belongs to a different table".

JOIN puts them back together

SELECT students.name, teachers.name
FROM students
JOIN teachers ON students.teacher_id = teachers.id;

Result:

Asha Mrs Rao
Ravi Mr Iyer
Meena Mrs Rao

The important part is ON students.teacher_id = teachers.id. That tells the database how the two tables connect — match a student to the teacher whose id equals that student's teacher_id.

How to read a JOIN, mechanically

Take it one student at a time:

Asha has teacher_id 1  ->  find teacher with id 1  ->  Mrs Rao   ->  join them
Ravi has teacher_id 2  ->  find teacher with id 2  ->  Mr Iyer   ->  join them
Meena has teacher_id 1 ->  find teacher with id 1  ->  Mrs Rao   ->  join them

The database builds a temporary combined table with columns from both, then your SELECT picks what to show from it.

Short names make joins readable

Writing students.name everywhere gets long. Give the tables short aliases:

SELECT s.name, t.name, t.subject
FROM students s
JOIN teachers t ON s.teacher_id = t.id;

students s means "call this table s from now on". Every real-world query does this. Notice you still need the prefix — both tables have a column called name, so s.name and t.name are how you say which one you mean.

Worked example 1 — join plus filter plus sort

SELECT s.name, s.marks, t.subject
FROM students s
JOIN teachers t ON s.teacher_id = t.id
WHERE s.marks > 70
ORDER BY s.marks DESC;

Everything you learned still works. The join simply gives you more columns to work with.

INNER JOIN and LEFT JOIN — the crucial difference

Plain JOIN is short for INNER JOIN. It keeps only rows that have a match on both sides.

So a student with no teacher assigned simply vanishes from the results. Often that is not what you want — you may not even notice they are missing.

SELECT s.name, t.name
FROM students s
LEFT JOIN teachers t ON s.teacher_id = t.id;

LEFT JOIN keeps every row from the left table (the one after FROM), whether or not it found a match. Where there is no match, the right-hand columns come back as NULL.

Asha    Mrs Rao
Ravi    Mr Iyer
Meena   Mrs Rao
Karthik None        <-- no teacher assigned, but the student still appears
This is the most consequential decision in a JOIN. Use INNER JOIN when a missing match means the row is irrelevant. Use LEFT JOIN when you need to see everything from the main table, including the gaps. Quietly losing rows to an INNER JOIN is one of the most common causes of wrong numbers in business reports — the total looks plausible, so nobody checks.

Worked example 2 — the "find what is missing" pattern

LEFT JOIN has a second use that is worth memorising as a shape. How do you find students with no teacher?

SELECT s.name
FROM students s
LEFT JOIN teachers t ON s.teacher_id = t.id
WHERE t.id IS NULL;

The logic: keep every student, then keep only those where the teacher side came back empty. Those are exactly the unmatched ones.

This pattern — LEFT JOIN then filter for NULL — answers a whole family of questions: customers who never ordered, products never sold, students not enrolled. Learn it as a shape and you will reuse it constantly.

Worked example 3 — joining then grouping

Everything combines. Here is how many students each teacher has:

SELECT t.name, COUNT(*) AS student_count
FROM students s
JOIN teachers t ON s.teacher_id = t.id
GROUP BY t.name
ORDER BY student_count DESC;

Result:

Mrs Rao 2
Mr Iyer 1

The database joins the tables first, then sorts the combined rows into piles by teacher name, then counts each pile. Every step you have learned, stacked together.

import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")

])

FROM students s
JOIN teachers t ON s.teacher_id = t.id
GROUP BY t.name
HAVING AVG(s.marks) > 70
"""

for row in db.execute(query):
    print(*row)

Written worksheet — 11 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Why is it a bad idea to store a teacher's name and subject inside the students table?
Show answer
It repeats the same information many times. When the teacher's details change you must update every copy, and if you miss one the data becomes permanently inconsistent - the same person recorded two different ways.
2
What is a primary key?
Show answer
The column that uniquely identifies a row in a table. Every table should have one.
3
What is a foreign key?
Show answer
A column that points at another table's primary key. 'Foreign' just means it belongs to a different table.
4
What does the ON part of a JOIN do?
Show answer
It tells the database how the two tables connect - which column in one table matches which column in the other.
5
Write a query joining orders to customers to show the customer name and order amount.
Show answer
SELECT c.name, o.amount FROM orders o JOIN customers c ON o.customer_id = c.id;
6
Why do we write 'FROM students s' instead of just 'FROM students'?
Show answer
It gives the table a short alias so you can write s.name instead of students.name. Real queries always do this because it keeps them readable.
7
What is the difference between INNER JOIN and LEFT JOIN?
Show answer
INNER JOIN keeps only rows that have a match on both sides. LEFT JOIN keeps every row from the left table, filling the right side with NULL where there is no match.
8
Why is quietly losing rows to an INNER JOIN dangerous in a business report?
Show answer
Because the total still looks plausible, so nobody checks. Rows silently disappear and the number is simply wrong.
9
Write the 'find what is missing' pattern to find customers who have never placed an order.
Show answer
SELECT c.name FROM customers c LEFT JOIN orders o ON o.customer_id = c.id WHERE o.id IS NULL;
10
Why must you write WHERE t.id IS NULL rather than WHERE t.id = NULL?
Show answer
Because NULL means 'unknown', and comparing an unknown to an unknown with = never returns true. SQL requires IS NULL / IS NOT NULL.
11
Write a query showing how many orders each customer has placed, most first.
Show answer
SELECT c.name, COUNT(*) AS n FROM orders o JOIN customers c ON o.customer_id = c.id GROUP BY c.name ORDER BY n DESC;
Lesson 19

Designing tables, and writing safe SQL

🗄️ SQL & Databases · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 12 worksheet questions

Creating a table

CREATE TABLE students (
    id       INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
    name     TEXT NOT NULL,
    marks    INTEGER,
    city     TEXT,
    email    TEXT UNIQUE
);

Each line is a column: its name, its type, and any rules attached.

The types you actually need

INTEGER    whole numbers
REAL       decimal numbers
TEXT       any text
DATE       a date, stored as 'YYYY-MM-DD'
BOOLEAN    true or false

Different database systems have slightly different type names, but these five cover the overwhelming majority of real columns.

Constraints — rules the database enforces for you

PRIMARY KEY     uniquely identifies the row; cannot be empty or duplicated
NOT NULL        this column must always have a value
UNIQUE          no two rows may have the same value here
DEFAULT 0       if nobody supplies a value, use this
CHECK (marks >= 0 AND marks <= 100)     the value must satisfy this
Put your rules in the database, not only in your program. If the rule lives only in your Python code, then a different script, a colleague's tool, or a manual fix can all bypass it and insert nonsense. A constraint in the database can never be bypassed by anything. It is the last line of defence, and it never gets tired or forgets.

Worked example 1 — a well-designed table

CREATE TABLE students (
    id         INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
    name       TEXT NOT NULL,
    email      TEXT UNIQUE,
    marks      INTEGER CHECK (marks >= 0 AND marks <= 100),
    city       TEXT,
    joined_at  DATE DEFAULT CURRENT_DATE
);

Read what this guarantees, without any application code being involved:

  • Every student has an id, and no two share it
  • Nobody can be inserted without a name
  • No two students can register the same email
  • Marks of 150 or -20 are rejected outright
  • The join date fills itself in automatically

Putting data in, changing it, removing it

INSERT INTO students (name, marks, city)
VALUES ('Asha', 88, 'Chennai');

UPDATE students
SET marks = 90
WHERE id = 1;

DELETE FROM students
WHERE id = 4;
The most expensive mistake in this entire course: forgetting the WHERE on an UPDATE or DELETE. 
UPDATE students SET marks = 90;    changes EVERY row in the table
DELETE FROM students;              deletes EVERY row
There is no undo. Professionals protect themselves with a habit: write the query as a SELECT first, look at exactly which rows come back, and only then change the word SELECT to UPDATE or DELETE. Do this every single time, even when you are certain. Especially when you are certain.

Indexes — why queries get slow, and how to fix it

Imagine finding a word in a 500-page book with no index. You read every page. That is what a database does without an index — it checks every single row. Fine for 100 rows, unusable for 10 million.

CREATE INDEX idx_students_city ON students(city);

An index is a sorted lookup structure the database maintains separately. Now finding all students in Chennai jumps straight to them instead of scanning everything.

What to index: columns you frequently use in WHERE, in JOIN ... ON, and in ORDER BY.

Why not index everything: every index must be updated on every insert, update and delete. So indexes make reads faster and writes slower, and they consume disk space. Index the columns you actually search on — not all of them.

To see what the database is really doing:

EXPLAIN QUERY PLAN
SELECT * FROM students WHERE city = 'Chennai';

If the output mentions SCAN on a large table, you are reading every row and probably need an index. If it says SEARCH ... USING INDEX, the index is being used.

Transactions — all or nothing

Consider moving 1000 rupees between two bank accounts. It takes two statements:

UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance - 1000 WHERE id = 1;
UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance + 1000 WHERE id = 2;

Now imagine the power fails between them. The money has left one account and arrived nowhere. It has simply vanished.

BEGIN;
    UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance - 1000 WHERE id = 1;
    UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance + 1000 WHERE id = 2;
COMMIT;

Wrapped in a transaction, both statements succeed together or neither takes effect at all. If anything fails in between, the database rolls the whole thing back as if it never started.

This guarantee is the main reason banks, hospitals and airlines run on relational databases rather than spreadsheets.

SQL injection — the security lesson you must not skip

Here is how a beginner builds a query from user input:

name = input("Name: ")
query = f"SELECT * FROM students WHERE name = '{name}'"     NEVER DO THIS

It works fine while people type ordinary names. Now suppose someone types:

' OR '1'='1

Your query becomes:

SELECT * FROM students WHERE name = '' OR '1'='1'

Since '1'='1' is always true, this returns every row in the table. The attacker has just read your entire student database.

Worse input is possible. A carefully crafted value can end your statement and start a new one — deleting tables, changing passwords, or extracting data.

The fix, which is easy

name = input("Name: ")
query = "SELECT * FROM students WHERE name = ?"
db.execute(query, (name,))                             SAFE

The ? is a placeholder. You hand the value separately, and the database treats it strictly as data — never as SQL to be executed. Someone typing ' OR '1'='1 simply gets no results, because no student is literally named that.

The rule, with no exceptions: never build SQL by joining strings together with user input. Always use placeholders. This vulnerability has been in the OWASP top ten of security risks for over twenty years and still causes major breaches every year — not because it is hard to prevent, but because people keep taking the shortcut. It takes the same amount of typing to do it correctly.

Where to go next with SQL

You now know enough to do real work. When you are ready for more:

  • Window functions — ranking rows within groups without collapsing them. This is the dividing line between a junior and a mid-level analyst.
  • CTEs (WITH ... AS) — naming a sub-query so complex logic reads clearly, top to bottom.
  • Subqueries — a query nested inside another.

All three build directly on what you have here. None of them make sense without it.

    id    INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,

    price REAL
)

db.execute("INSERT INTO books (id, title, price) VALUES (1, 'Wings of Fire', 250.0)")

    print(*row)

""")

db.execute("INSERT INTO books (id, title, price) VALUES (1, 'Wings of Fire', 250.0)")

for row in db.execute("SELECT title, price FROM books"):
    print(*row)
2
Add a CHECK so marks must be between 0 and 100. The bad insert is already wrapped in try/except — run it and confirm the database rejects 150.
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")

db.execute("""
CREATE TABLE students (
    id    INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
    name  TEXT NOT NULL,
    marks INTEGER ____ (marks >= 0 AND marks <= 100)
)
""")

db.execute("INSERT INTO students VALUES (1, 'Asha', 88)")

try:
    db.execute("INSERT INTO students VALUES (2, 'Bad', 150)")
    print("accepted 150 - constraint missing")
except Exception:
    print("rejected 150")

for row in db.execute("SELECT name, marks FROM students"):
    print(*row)
Show hint
The keyword is CHECK
Show solution
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")

db.execute("""
CREATE TABLE students (
    id    INTEGER PRIMARY KEY,
    name  TEXT NOT NULL,
    marks INTEGER CHECK (marks >= 0 AND marks <= 100)
)
""")

db.execute("INSERT INTO students VALUES (1, 'Asha', 88)")

try:
    db.execute("INSERT INTO students VALUES (2, 'Bad', 150)")
    print("accepted 150 - constraint missing")
except Exception:
    print("rejected 150")

for row in db.execute("SELECT name, marks FROM students"):
    print(*row)
3
UPDATE Asha's marks to 95 — and only Asha's. Then show everyone.
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, marks INT)")
db.executemany("INSERT INTO students VALUES (?,?,?)", [
    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65),
])

db.execute("UPDATE students SET marks = 95 ____")

for row in db.execute("SELECT name, marks FROM students ORDER BY id"):
    print(*row)
Show hint
WHERE name = 'Asha' — without the WHERE, everyone gets 95.
Show solution
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, marks INT)")
db.executemany("INSERT INTO students VALUES (?,?,?)", [
    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65),
])

db.execute("UPDATE students SET marks = 95 WHERE name = 'Asha'")

for row in db.execute("SELECT name, marks FROM students ORDER BY id"):
    print(*row)
4
See the danger for yourself. This UPDATE has no WHERE. Run it and look at what happened to Ravi.

])

    print(*row)
Show hint
Just run it. Every row changed. There is no undo for this in a real database.
Show solution
import sqlite3

    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65),

Create an index on the city column, then confirm it exists.
import sqlite3

db.execute("CREATE ____ idx_students_city ON students(city)")

for row in db.execute("SELECT name FROM sqlite_master WHERE type='index'"):
    print(*row)
Show hint
CREATE INDEX idx_students_city ON students(city)
Show solution
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, city TEXT)")

db.execute("CREATE INDEX idx_students_city ON students(city)")

for row in db.execute("SELECT name FROM sqlite_master WHERE type='index'"):
    print(*row)
7
See the SQL injection attack work. Run this unsafe code and notice it returns every student, not one.
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, marks INT)")
db.executemany("INSERT INTO students VALUES (?,?,?)", [
    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65), (3, "Meena", 92),
])

attacker_input = "' OR '1'='1"

unsafe = "SELECT name FROM students WHERE name = '" + attacker_input + "'"

for row in db.execute(unsafe):
    print(*row)
Show hint
Just run it. All three names appear - the attacker read the whole table.
Show solution
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, marks INT)")
db.executemany("INSERT INTO students VALUES (?,?,?)", [
    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65), (3, "Meena", 92),
])

attacker_input = "' OR '1'='1"

unsafe = "SELECT name FROM students WHERE name = '" + attacker_input + "'"

for row in db.execute(unsafe):
    print(*row)
8
Challenge: now fix it with a placeholder. The same attack input should return nothing, so only the word safe is printed.
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, marks INT)")
db.executemany("INSERT INTO students VALUES (?,?,?)", [
    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65), (3, "Meena", 92),
])

attacker_input = "' OR '1'='1"

safe = "SELECT name FROM students WHERE name = ____"

for row in db.execute(safe, (attacker_input,)):
    print(*row)

print("safe")
Show hint
Replace the blank with a single ? — the value is passed separately as the second argument.
Show solution
import sqlite3
db = sqlite3.connect(":memory:")
db.execute("CREATE TABLE students (id INT, name TEXT, marks INT)")
db.executemany("INSERT INTO students VALUES (?,?,?)", [
    (1, "Asha", 88), (2, "Ravi", 65), (3, "Meena", 92),
])

attacker_input = "' OR '1'='1"

safe = "SELECT name FROM students WHERE name = ?"

for row in db.execute(safe, (attacker_input,)):
    print(*row)

print("safe")

Written worksheet — 12 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Name the five column types you will use most often.
Show answer
INTEGER (whole numbers) REAL (decimals) TEXT (any text) DATE (stored as YYYY-MM-DD) BOOLEAN (true/false)
2
What do PRIMARY KEY, NOT NULL and UNIQUE each guarantee?
Show answer
PRIMARY KEY - uniquely identifies the row, cannot be empty or duplicated NOT NULL - this column must always have a value UNIQUE - no two rows may share this value
3
Why put rules in the database rather than only in your program code?
Show answer
A rule in your code can be bypassed by a different script, a colleague's tool, or a manual fix. A database constraint can never be bypassed by anything. It is the last line of defence and it never forgets.
4
Write a CREATE TABLE for products with an id, a name that cannot be empty, and a price that cannot be negative.
Show answer
CREATE TABLE products ( id INTEGER PRIMARY KEY, name TEXT NOT NULL, price REAL CHECK (price >= 0) );
5
What happens if you run: UPDATE students SET marks = 90;
Show answer
Every single row in the table is changed to 90. There is no undo. This is the most expensive mistake in the whole course.
6
What habit protects you from that mistake?
Show answer
Write the query as a SELECT first. Look at exactly which rows come back. Only then change the word SELECT to UPDATE or DELETE. Do it every time, especially when you feel certain.
7
What is an index and why does it make queries faster?
Show answer
A sorted lookup structure the database maintains separately. Without one, the database reads every row - like finding a word in a book with no index. With one, it jumps straight to the matching rows.
8
Which columns should you index, and why not index everything?
Show answer
Index columns used in WHERE, JOIN ON, and ORDER BY. Do not index everything because every index must be updated on every write, so indexes make reads faster but writes slower and use disk space.
9
What is a transaction and why do banks need them?
Show answer
A group of statements that all succeed together or none take effect. Moving money is two updates - if the power fails between them, money would vanish. A transaction guarantees that cannot happen.
10
Explain SQL injection. What does typing ' OR '1'='1 achieve?
Show answer
It closes the quote early and adds a condition that is always true. The query becomes WHERE name = '' OR '1'='1' which matches every row, so the attacker reads the entire table.
11
Write the safe version of a query that looks up a student by name.
Show answer
query = "SELECT * FROM students WHERE name = ?" db.execute(query, (name,)) The ? is a placeholder. The value is passed separately and treated strictly as data, never as SQL.
12
Why is SQL injection still causing breaches after twenty years?
Show answer
Not because it is hard to prevent - the safe version takes the same amount of typing. People keep taking the shortcut of building query strings by joining text together.
STAGE 4

📊 Data Analysis

Take messy real data, clean it honestly, find something true in it, and report it without misleading anybody. This plus SQL is a Data Analyst job.

1 tracks · 4 lessons · 32 exercises

📊 Data Analysis

Take messy real data, clean it, find something true in it, and report it honestly. This plus SQL is a Data Analyst job.

By the end of this track you will be able to:
  • Load and inspect a table of records before touching it
  • Find and fix the six things wrong with real data
  • Group and summarise to answer actual questions
  • Choose the right chart and avoid misleading anybody
  • Know the pandas method for everything you write by hand
Lesson 20

Looking at data before you touch it

📊 Data Analysis · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

The mistake almost everybody makes

You get a data file. You open it, immediately start calculating averages, and produce a report.

Two weeks later someone notices the average salary is 4 crore rupees, because one row had an extra zero. Your report went to the whole department. Nobody checked, because it looked professional.

Always look at the data before you analyse it. Not a glance — a deliberate inspection. This lesson is that inspection.

The shape of tabular data in Python

You already know it from Stage 2 — a list of dictionaries:

records = [
    {"name": "Asha",  "marks": 88, "city": "Chennai"},
    {"name": "Ravi",  "marks": 65, "city": "Pune"},
]

This is the same shape as a database table and the same shape as a spreadsheet. One row per record, the same fields on each.

What real analysts use: pandas

pandas is a Python library built exactly for this. Its main object is the DataFrame — a table with named columns.

import pandas as pd

df = pd.read_csv("students.csv")     load a file into a DataFrame

df.head()        first 5 rows        ALWAYS start here
df.shape         (rows, columns)
df.info()        column types and how many values are missing
df.describe()    min, max, mean, quartiles for numeric columns
Those four commands are the inspection ritual. Run all four, in that order, on every dataset you ever receive, before writing a single line of analysis. It takes thirty seconds and it catches most disasters.

What each one tells you

head() — do the columns contain what their names promise? Is a "date" column actually text like "12/3/24"? Are there obvious junk rows at the top?

shape — is the row count what you expected? If you were told 5,000 students and see 4,213, something was lost before it reached you. Ask before proceeding.

info() — which columns have missing values, and how many? A column that is 60% empty usually cannot be used at all.

describe() — this is where the extra zero shows up. If max salary is 40,000,000 and the 75th percentile is 60,000, you have found your problem.

Worked example 1 — inspecting by hand

Here is the same inspection in plain Python, so you can see exactly what pandas is doing for you:

records = [
    {"name": "Asha",  "marks": 88, "city": "Chennai"},
    {"name": "Ravi",  "marks": 65, "city": "Pune"},
    {"name": "Meena", "marks": 92, "city": "Chennai"},
]

print("rows:", len(records))
print("columns:", list(records[0].keys()))

marks = [r["marks"] for r in records]
print("min:", min(marks))
print("max:", max(marks))
print("mean:", round(sum(marks) / len(marks), 1))

Output:

rows: 3
columns: ['name', 'marks', 'city']
min: 65
max: 92
mean: 81.7

That is shape and describe(), done by hand. pandas just does it in one line for a hundred columns at once.

Counting missing values

Missing data is the single most common problem you will face. Find it first:

records = [
    {"name": "Asha",  "marks": 88,   "phone": "9876543210"},
    {"name": "Ravi",  "marks": None, "phone": None},
    {"name": "Meena", "marks": 92,   "phone": None},
]

for column in ["marks", "phone"]:
    missing = 0
    for r in records:
        if r[column] is None:
            missing = missing + 1
    print(column, "missing:", missing)

Output:

marks missing: 1
phone missing: 2

In pandas this is df.isnull().sum() — one line, every column at once. Same idea.

Note is None, not == None. This is the same distinction as SQL's IS NULL versus = NULL from the last stage. Python is more forgiving here than SQL, but is None is the correct form and you should use it.

Finding outliers

An outlier is a value far outside the normal range. Some are real (one genuinely brilliant student). Some are errors (an extra zero). You must look at each and decide which.

salaries = [45000, 52000, 48000, 61000, 4500000, 55000]

avg = sum(salaries) / len(salaries)
print("mean:", round(avg))

ordered = sorted(salaries)
middle = ordered[len(ordered) // 2]
print("median:", middle)

Output:

mean: 786833
median: 55000
This gap is your alarm bell. When the mean is wildly higher than the median, a small number of very large values are dragging it up. Nobody in that list earns anywhere near 786,833. The median of 55,000 describes the group honestly; the mean actively misleads.

The rule: whenever mean and median differ a lot, investigate before reporting either one.

The six things wrong with real data

Check for all six, every time:

  • Missing values — empty cells, None, or placeholder text like "N/A" and "-"
  • Duplicates — the same record entered twice
  • Wrong types — numbers stored as text, dates stored as text
  • Inconsistent text — "Chennai", "chennai", " Chennai " all meaning the same city
  • Outliers — the extra zero, the impossible age of 200
  • Impossible values — negative marks, a future date of birth, 130% attendance

The next lesson fixes all six. This lesson is about finding them first.

Why must you inspect data before analysing it?

Because errors like an extra zero produce results that look professional but are completely wrong. Nobody downstream checks. Thirty seconds of inspection prevents this.

Name the four pandas inspection commands and what each reveals.

You were told the file has 5,000 rows. df.shape says 4,213. What should you do?

Lesson 21

Cleaning messy data

📊 Data Analysis · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

This is 80% of the job

People imagine data analysis is building clever models. In reality most of your time goes on cleaning. This is not a failure of the profession — it is the profession. Clean data honestly and the analysis is often straightforward.

Last lesson found the six problems. This one fixes them.

Problem 1 — inconsistent text

You met this in Stage 2. It matters even more here, because inconsistent text silently splits your groups:

cities = ["Chennai", "chennai", " Chennai ", "CHENNAI"]

Group by city and you get four groups of one instead of one group of four. Every number in your report is then wrong, and it will look completely plausible.

clean = [c.strip().title() for c in cities]
print(clean)

Output: ['Chennai', 'Chennai', 'Chennai', 'Chennai']

In pandas: df["city"] = df["city"].str.strip().str.title()

Problem 2 — wrong types

Numbers arriving as text is constant, especially from spreadsheets and web forms:

raw = ["88", "65", "92"]
marks = [int(m) for m in raw]
print(sum(marks))          245

But real data has broken entries mixed in, and int() will crash on them:

raw = ["88", "N/A", "65", "", "92"]

marks = []
for m in raw:
    try:
        marks.append(int(m))
    except ValueError:
        pass        skip anything that will not convert

print(marks)               [88, 65, 92]

In pandas: pd.to_numeric(df["marks"], errors="coerce") — the coerce turns unconvertible values into missing rather than crashing.

Never silently drop data without counting it. If you started with 5,000 rows and your clean list has 3,100, you must know that and say so. "1,900 rows had unreadable marks and were excluded" belongs in your report. Quietly losing 38% of the data and presenting the rest as complete is the difference between analysis and misinformation.

Problem 3 — missing values

You have three honest options, and choosing between them is a judgement call you must be able to defend:

  • Drop the row — fine when few rows are affected and the field is essential.
  • Fill with the median — reasonable for numbers when you need to keep the row.
  • Leave it missing and report it — often the most honest choice.
values = [88, None, 92, None, 70]

present = [v for v in values if v is not None]
median = sorted(present)[len(present) // 2]

filled = [v if v is not None else median for v in values]
print(filled)

Output: [88, 88, 92, 88, 70]

Use the median, not the mean. One outlier of 4,500,000 would drag the mean up and you would then insert that wrong number into every gap, spreading the error through your data.

In pandas: df["marks"].fillna(df["marks"].median())

Problem 4 — duplicates

records = [
    {"id": 1, "name": "Asha"},
    {"id": 2, "name": "Ravi"},
    {"id": 1, "name": "Asha"},
]

seen = set()
unique = []
for r in records:
    if r["id"] not in seen:
        seen.add(r["id"])
        unique.append(r)

print(len(unique))         2

This is the "remember what you have seen" pattern from Stage 1's data structures lesson. In pandas: df.drop_duplicates(subset=["id"])

Decide what "duplicate" means before you deduplicate. Same id? Same name and date of birth? Two people genuinely can share a name. Removing rows on the wrong definition destroys real data, and you will not get it back.

Problem 5 — impossible values

records = [
    {"name": "Asha",  "marks": 88},
    {"name": "Ravi",  "marks": 150},
    {"name": "Meena", "marks": -5},
]

valid = [r for r in records if 0 <= r["marks"] <= 100]
print(len(valid), "valid,", len(records) - len(valid), "rejected")

Output: 1 valid, 2 rejected

Notice 0 <= r["marks"] <= 100. Python lets you chain comparisons like this, and it reads exactly like mathematics.

Problem 6 — outliers

Outliers need a decision, not an automatic rule. For each one ask: is this real, or is it an error?

  • A salary of 4,500,000 among salaries of 50,000 — almost certainly an extra zero. Investigate, then fix or remove.
  • A student scoring 100 when others score 60 — probably real. Keep it.

If you cannot tell, report both figures: "average 55,000, or 786,833 including one exceptional value". Let the reader decide, and be transparent about what you did.

Worked example — a complete cleaning pipeline

raw = [
    {"name": "  asha ", "city": "chennai", "marks": "88"},
    {"name": "RAVI",    "city": " Pune",   "marks": "N/A"},
    {"name": "Meena",   "city": "CHENNAI", "marks": "92"},
]

clean = []
rejected = 0

for r in raw:
    try:
        marks = int(r["marks"])
    except ValueError:
        rejected = rejected + 1
        continue                      skip this row entirely

    if not (0 <= marks <= 100):
        rejected = rejected + 1
        continue

    clean.append({
        "name":  r["name"].strip().title(),
        "city":  r["city"].strip().title(),
        "marks": marks,
    })

print("kept:", len(clean), "rejected:", rejected)
for c in clean:
    print(c["name"], c["city"], c["marks"])

Output:

kept: 2 rejected: 1
Asha Chennai 88
Meena Chennai 92

Note the two things this does that a careless version would not: it counts what it rejected, and it uses continue to skip cleanly rather than building a half-cleaned record.

Write down every cleaning decision as you make it. "Dropped 1,900 rows with unreadable marks. Filled 43 missing ages with the median. Removed 12 duplicate ids." This list goes in your README and at the front of your report. It is what separates an analyst people trust from one they do not — and the first question a good interviewer asks about any project is what you did with the messy rows.

Written worksheet — 10 questions

Answer these on paper, in your own words. Writing an explanation out is the fastest way to find out whether you actually understood it.

1
Roughly how much of a data analyst's time goes on cleaning, and why is that not a failure?
Show answer
About 80%. It is not a failure - it IS the profession. Clean the data honestly and the analysis is often straightforward.
2
Why does inconsistent text like 'Chennai' / 'chennai' / ' Chennai ' matter so much?
Show answer
Grouping splits them into separate groups. You get four groups of one instead of one group of four, and every number in your report is wrong while looking completely plausible.
3
Write the standard fix for inconsistent text.
Show answer
[c.strip().title() for c in cities] In pandas: df["city"].str.strip().str.title()
4
Why must you count rows you drop rather than silently skipping them?
Show answer
If you start with 5,000 and end with 3,100, that must be reported. Quietly losing 38% and presenting the rest as complete is misinformation, not analysis.
5
What are the three honest options for missing values?
Show answer
1. Drop the row - when few are affected and the field is essential 2. Fill with the median - for numbers when you need to keep the row 3. Leave it missing and report it - often the most honest
6
Why fill missing numbers with the median rather than the mean?
Show answer
One outlier drags the mean up. You would then insert that wrong number into every gap, spreading the error through your data.
7
What must you decide before removing duplicates?
Show answer
What 'duplicate' actually means - same id? same name and date of birth? Two people genuinely can share a name. The wrong definition destroys real data permanently.
8
How do you decide what to do with an outlier?
Show answer
Ask whether it is real or an error. A salary 100x the others is probably an extra zero - investigate. A student scoring 100 when others score 60 is probably real - keep it. If you cannot tell, report both figures and be transparent.
9
Write the check for a mark being in a valid range using Python's chained comparison.
Show answer
if 0 <= marks <= 100:
10
What should go in your report about cleaning, and why does it matter?
Show answer
Every decision: 'Dropped 1,900 rows with unreadable marks. Filled 43 missing ages with the median. Removed 12 duplicate ids.' It separates an analyst people trust from one they do not - and it is the first thing a good interviewer asks about.
Lesson 22

Grouping, summarising, answering questions

📊 Data Analysis · 35 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

You already know this shape

In SQL you wrote:

SELECT city, AVG(marks) FROM students GROUP BY city;

The Python equivalent is the same idea, written by hand. And in pandas it is one line again. Three ways to express one concept — worth seeing all three, because you will meet all three at work.

Grouping by hand

records = [
    {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 88},
    {"city": "Pune",    "marks": 65},
    {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 92},
    {"city": "Pune",    "marks": 78},
]

groups = {}
for r in records:
    city = r["city"]
    if city not in groups:
        groups[city] = []
    groups[city].append(r["marks"])

print(groups)

Output: {'Chennai': [88, 92], 'Pune': [65, 78]}

This is literally the "piles" picture from the SQL lesson, built as a real dictionary. Each key is a pile; each value is the list of things in it.

There is a shorter way using setdefault:

for r in records:
    groups.setdefault(r["city"], []).append(r["marks"])

Read it as: get the list for this city, creating an empty one if it does not exist, then append to it.

Summarising each group

for city in sorted(groups):
    values = groups[city]
    avg = sum(values) / len(values)
    print(f"{city:<10} {len(values):>3} students  avg {avg:.1f}")

Output:

Chennai      2 students  avg 90.0
Pune         2 students  avg 71.5

Notice the formatting from Stage 2 doing its job — the columns line up and it reads like a report rather than raw output.

In pandas this whole thing is:

df.groupby("city")["marks"].agg(["count", "mean"])

Worked example 1 — finding the top group

best_city = None
best_avg = -1

for city, values in groups.items():
    avg = sum(values) / len(values)
    if avg > best_avg:
        best_avg = avg
        best_city = city

print(f"{best_city} leads with {best_avg:.1f}")

Output: Chennai leads with 90.0

The pattern — track the best so far, replace it when you find better — is the same one you used to find the highest mark in Stage 1. It works on anything.

Grouping by two things

Real questions often need two dimensions: "average marks by city and by year".

The trick is to make the key a tuple:

records = [
    {"city": "Chennai", "year": 1, "marks": 88},
    {"city": "Chennai", "year": 2, "marks": 92},
    {"city": "Pune",    "year": 1, "marks": 65},
]

groups = {}
for r in records:
    key = (r["city"], r["year"])
    groups.setdefault(key, []).append(r["marks"])

for key in sorted(groups):
    city, year = key
    values = groups[key]
    print(city, year, sum(values) / len(values))

Output:

Chennai 1 88.0
Chennai 2 92.0
Pune 1 65.0

A tuple can be a dictionary key because it cannot be changed — the same immutability idea you met with strings in Stage 2. A list cannot be a key, for exactly that reason.

Percentages — and the mistake to avoid

total = len(records)
for city, values in groups.items():
    share = len(values) / total * 100
    print(f"{city}: {share:.1f}%")
Always report the count alongside the percentage. "67% of students prefer this" sounds compelling. "67% of students prefer this (2 out of 3)" tells the truth. Percentages on small numbers are close to meaningless, and quoting them without the base is one of the most common ways honest people mislead their readers by accident.

Worked example 2 — answering a real question

Question: which city has the highest pass rate, and is the difference meaningful?

records = [
    {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 88}, {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 35},
    {"city": "Pune",    "marks": 65}, {"city": "Pune",    "marks": 78},
    {"city": "Pune",    "marks": 45},
]

groups = {}
for r in records:
    groups.setdefault(r["city"], []).append(r["marks"])

for city in sorted(groups):
    values = groups[city]
    passed = len([v for v in values if v >= 40])
    rate = passed / len(values) * 100
    print(f"{city}: {passed}/{len(values)} passed ({rate:.0f}%)")

Output:

Chennai: 1/2 passed (50%)
Pune: 3/3 passed (100%)

Now the honest interpretation. Pune looks twice as good. But these are 2 and 3 students. One different student would flip the answer completely. The correct report says: "Pune 100% (3 of 3), Chennai 50% (1 of 2) — sample sizes are far too small to draw any conclusion."

Knowing when your data cannot answer the question is a more valuable skill than any calculation. It is also the thing that separates analysts who get trusted from ones who get quietly ignored.

    rows.append((avg, city, len(values), passed))

rows.sort(reverse=True)
for avg, city, n, passed in rows:
    print(f"{city} {n} {avg:.1f} {passed / n * 100:.0f}%")

Lesson 23

Charts that tell the truth

📊 Data Analysis · 30 min read · 8 exercises · 10 worksheet questions

A chart is an argument

When you draw a chart you are making a claim about what the data means. That gives you a responsibility, because charts are believed far more readily than tables of numbers. People trust pictures.

You can mislead badly without writing a single false number. This lesson is about not doing that, accidentally or otherwise.

Choosing the right chart

  • Bar chart — comparing categories. Marks by city, sales by product. The most useful chart there is.
  • Line chart — change over time, and only over time. Never use a line for categories; a line implies the points connect meaningfully.
  • Histogram — the distribution of one number. How many students scored 0-10, 10-20, and so on. Shows you the shape of your data.
  • Scatter plot — the relationship between two numbers. Attendance against marks.
  • Box plot — spread and outliers at a glance.
  • Pie chart — almost never. Humans compare angles badly. Use a bar chart. The only reasonable case is two or three parts of a clear whole.

The four ways charts lie

1. Truncated y-axis

Two bars, 51% and 49%. Start the axis at 48% and one bar looks three times taller than the other. The numbers are correct; the picture is a lie.

Bar charts must start at zero. Always. This is not a stylistic preference — the length of a bar is what encodes the value, so a bar not starting at zero is drawing the wrong length. Line charts showing change over time may start elsewhere, but say so clearly.

2. Hidden sample size

A bar showing "City A: 100% pass rate" is technically accurate when 3 students sat the exam. Put the count on the chart, or in the label.

3. Cherry-picked range

Showing 2019-2021 when the data runs 2015-2024, because those three years support your point. If you shorten the range, say why.

4. Implying cause

Ice cream sales and drowning deaths rise together. Ice cream does not cause drowning — hot weather causes both. A chart of two rising lines invites the reader to assume one caused the other. Correlation is not causation, and this is the single most common error in published analysis.

The test to apply before you publish: if someone who disagreed with your conclusion looked at this chart, would they say it was fair? If you find yourself hoping nobody checks the axis, or hoping nobody asks how many people were surveyed, you already know the answer.

Drawing charts in Python

import matplotlib.pyplot as plt

cities = ["Chennai", "Pune", "Delhi"]
averages = [90.0, 71.5, 45.0]

fig, ax = plt.subplots(figsize=(8, 5))
ax.bar(cities, averages)

ax.set_title("Average marks by city (n=5 students)")
ax.set_ylabel("Average marks (out of 100)")
ax.set_ylim(0, 100)              start at zero, end at the real maximum

plt.tight_layout()
plt.savefig("chart.png", dpi=150)

Three things in there that most beginners omit and that matter:

  • set_ylim(0, 100) — starts at zero and ends at the true maximum, so 90 looks like 90% of the way up rather than filling the frame
  • The sample size in the title
  • The unit in the axis label — "Average marks" alone does not say out of what

Text charts, which you can build right now

You do not need a plotting library to see the shape of your data. This works anywhere:

data = {"Chennai": 90, "Pune": 71, "Delhi": 45}

for city in sorted(data, key=data.get, reverse=True):
    value = data[city]
    bar = "#" * (value // 5)
    print(f"{city:<10} {bar} {value}")

Output:

Chennai    ################## 90
Pune       ############## 71
Delhi      ######### 45

The bars start at zero because they begin at the left edge — a text chart cannot easily lie about its axis, which is a small point in its favour.

sorted(data, key=data.get, reverse=True) sorts the keys by their values, highest first. data.get is being handed over as the function to look up each key by.

Writing the report

A report is not a pile of charts. Structure it:

  • The question you set out to answer, stated plainly
  • What you did to the data — every cleaning decision, every row you dropped
  • What you found — with numbers and sample sizes
  • What you cannot conclude — the limits of the data
  • What you would do next with more time or better data

That fourth point is the one people skip, and the one that most builds trust. An analyst who says "this sample is too small to be confident" is worth ten who never mention it.

Worked example — an honest summary

records = [
    {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 88}, {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 92},
    {"city": "Pune", "marks": 65}, {"city": "Pune", "marks": 35},
    {"city": "Pune", "marks": 78},
]

groups = {}
for r in records:
    groups.setdefault(r["city"], []).append(r["marks"])

print("AVERAGE MARKS BY CITY")
print("total students:", len(records))
print()

for city in sorted(groups, key=lambda c: -sum(groups[c]) / len(groups[c])):
    values = groups[city]
    avg = sum(values) / len(values)
    bar = "#" * int(avg // 5)
    print(f"{city:<10} {bar} {avg:.1f}  (n={len(values)})")

print()
print("NOTE: sample sizes of 2 and 3 are too small for a reliable comparison.")

Output:

AVERAGE MARKS BY CITY
total students: 5

Chennai    ################## 90.0  (n=2)
Pune       ############ 59.3  (n=3)

NOTE: sample sizes of 2 and 3 are too small for a reliable comparison.

Every number is there, the sample size is on every row, and the caveat is stated rather than buried. That is what an honest report looks like, and it took no more effort than a dishonest one.

    {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 88}, {"city": "Chennai", "marks": 92},
    {"city": "Pune", "marks": 65}, {"city": "Pune", "marks": 35},
    {"city": "Pune", "marks": 78},
